SAN FRANCISCO. WEDNESDAY, JULY 12, 1899.
THE CALL CARRIES AID TO MAROONED EGG HUNTERS.
When it was made public that two egg hunters. Frank Martin and Joe Costa, were marooned
on the bleak and inhospitable rocks of North Farallon Island, and had been for weeks without
water or food other than the eggs they had purposed to steal. The, Call lost no time in idle specu
lation as to the chances of the men being rescued, but immediately sent out the tug Reliance fully
provisioned in aid of the castaways. Notwithstanding the fact that the waves were running so high
that it was almost worth a man's life to attempt to approach the island in a boat, the sturdy and
brave men in charge of the tug succeeded in getting provisions and water to the suffering men. and
left them with the assurance that they would be looked after and taken off as soon as the waves went
down sufficiently to permit a landing.
Three times had the employers of the men attempted to succor them, and each time tnÂ°ir
efforts were made unavailing by the force of the storm. Three battered and stranded boats decaying
on the drear rocks tell the story of those attempts, but The Call's boat successfully fulfilled its mis
sion, and while the other papers of the city were telling the public that unless something ware done
quickly the men would die, The Call was landing provisions and water to the almost famished unfor
tunates.
risk, will probably reach the city
in the Ma Belle to-day or to-mor
row.

The raiding became so great that

tion of one individual, who was allowed

this restriction did not give the desired
relief, so a few years ago the taking :
of eggs from the rocks was entirely !
prohibited.
When seal hunting was prohibited by j

and when the taking of eggs from the
Farallon rocks was stopped egg poach

I for "Farallon eggs" opened and Frank ]

A drearier or more desolate spot

| Coast. During every storm It is swept
to its highest pinnacle by driv
ing spray and only in a per
j feet calm is it possible to land
i^on or leave the rock. Millions of sea.
fowl and thousands of sea lions make
their home (here, and as this is the
breeding season the lions are very fero- ]
cious. Several of them attacked the j
; egg hunters and on one occasion Joe j

hy the leg, threw him down. and then !
1 mounted guard over him. Martin '
I grabbed an iron rod used in climbing
! the ciiff arid went to his companion's
assistance. A vigorous poke in the eye
caused the 11 to lose its balance and
go tumbling Into the breakers below.
This desolate island was the spot
i upon which Martin and Costa were to
j live until they had gathered in 5000
j dozen of eggs. Had all gone well no
i one would have known where the mar
ket supply came from, but then all did
not go well. The men were put on the
j rock in a dead calm and with provi
j sions sufficient to last them a week.
I For several days the calm continued
i and when the Ma Belle returned to the
city she carried 120 dozen of eggs with
her. On taking his leave Captain Bob
S Spear promised to return with water
I and provisions inside of four days. The
Ma Belle was back on the appointed
date, but in the meantime a storm had

As the sloop disappeared in the dis

In. hope, subsisting on raw eggs alone..

While the Ma Belle was In San Fran
cisco being repaired and made ready
for a third attempt at rescue, the light
keepers on the South Farallon got an
inkling that all was not right on the
most northerly rock. From a remark
dropped by Spear they came to the con
clusion that some men were weather
bound on the rock, so on the first fine
day they launched a boat, provisioned
it with water, bacon, potatoes, onions,
two rabbits, several loaves of bread and
whatever else they could spare. When
they reached the rock Martin and Costa
were almost dead from thirst and ex
posure.
The lighthouse men are experts at
rigging up a trolley from the shore to a
boat, and it was not long before the
things they had brought with them
were passing over a heaving line to the
shore. Some charcoal brought from the
lighthouse was also sent ashore, and
with it Costa did not take long to make
a fire. The weather was such that the
men would have risked their lives to
leave the rock, but on receiving this
welcome installment of aid, they waved
farewell to the lightkeepers and turned
to the first cooked meal they had seen
in weeks.
This was a fortnight ago and the sup
ply of provisions given them by the
lightkeepers lasted only a little over a
week. Again they were reduced to an
; egg diet, and until The Call's relief ex
pedition arrived eggs had been meat
i and drink to them. It was easy to ap-
I pease their hunger.' but they were al
ways thirsty and toward the end the
desire for a drink of water was almost
maddening.
When the tug Reliance approached
! the rock they pointed to their parched
! throats and motioned that water was
j all they wanted. It was finally got
ashore to them in a five-gallon milk
; can and those poor fellows drank until
they could not hold the can to their
lips any longer. They were offered a
passage back to the city, but refused,
saying they had suffered the tortures
of the damned in order to secure the
eggs they went out for and now they
were going to take them to the city or
stay on the rock forever.
Enough canned meats, hardtack and
bread to last them a week were put
ashore. This will be more than enough
to last them, as the sea was rapidly
going down yesterday and by to-day or
to-morrow the Ma Belle will be able
to take them and their eggs off the
rock.
It was 6 o'clock Tuesday morning
when the tug Reliance with two Call
men aboard started for the North Far
j allon rock. Captain Gilbert Brokaw
i had made all the necessary arrange
ments and was ready for any emerg
ency. The skiff Vigilant was lashed on
deck and in it Captain Brokaw' said his
mate. J. Olsson, could make a landing
anywhere.
No one on the Reliance knew on
which of the three 'rocks forming the
North Farallones the' men were, so a
systematic search had to be made.
They were discovered on the most
northerly one of the group. A rope fas
tened to the pinnacle of the rock and
falling to a landing place near the base
revealed their whereabouts to Captain
Brokaw. On this spot were collected
! thousands of eggs, some in sacks and
! some in tin cans, but the majority lay
loose on the rock. The rope had been
used in scaling the rock to secure the
eggs.
Martin and Costa had been sheltered
from the wind in a fissure of the rock,
but a hail from the tug soon brought
them forth, when they made a panto
mime appeal for water. The skiff Vigi
lant was lanched from the deck of the
Reliance and a five-gallon can of water
j put in it. Mate Olsson and one of The
Call men got aboard and started for
the rock. It was impossible to make
a landing owing to the swell, so Mar
tin threw a rope to the boat and Olsson
| made it fast to the milk can. To make
I sure there would be no mishap, he fas
tened a second line to the can and held
it in his hand, paying out as Martin
hauled in. It was lucky he did so. as
the line Martin was hauling on parted
and both can and water would have
been lost.
The precious can was hauled back
aboard the skiff and again Martin
threw his line aboard and again it was
made fast to the can. This time Costa
got down on the rock and kept it from
chafing, so the water reached the rock
in safety. It did not take long to g<\
the lid off and then each man took a
long draught. As long as their weak
ened arms could hold the can up they
kept it to their lins, only to sit down

Both men were emaciated, but not as !

By very careful handling Mate Ols-
Fon kept the skiff near enough to the
rocks to carry on a conversation. It
was not carried on in a stage whisper
and was occasionally drowned by the
"squawk! squawk!" of the myriad of
birds that circled overhead and the

"That is the first drink of water we
have had in five days," said Martin in
answer to a question. "We have used
eggs as meat and drink so long that j
the sight of one almost nauseates me
and I will never be able to look at a
murre again without disgust. We have
had several fights with the sea lions, j
and on Saturday, Sunday and Monday
we were drenched again and again with
the driving spray. We live in a hole

and we have not had a bit of fire since
the men from the lighthouse station
brought us some coke along with a sup

"No, we won't go back wijth you,
thanks just the same. I am not sur
prised that The Call Bent you nut to

you see the Ma Belle will be here as
soon as the swejl goes down and that
will be to-morrow, I think, and we are
bound to get these eggs to market. We
have been out here a mighty long time
and Buffered considerable getting these
things gathered, and if we go away
now all our time and labor will be
wasted. We wouldn'.t have a cent to
show for all we have gone through. A
wash and a clean bed to Bleep in would
be worth more than the money, but
now we have stayed this long with the
venture we'll stay to the end.
"ThÂ« first time he was out here in
the Ma Belle, Spear stayed six days,
trying to make a landing, and the next
time he stayed a week. I never saw
such a run of bad weather and we got
the full benefit of it."
Just about this time the skiff Vigi
lant began to leak considerably and the
bailer had to be used freely. Mate Ols
son therefore roved back to the Re
liance and got the provisions Captain
Brokaw had fixed up. These were
taken back to the rock and safely got

in getting the Vigilant back aboard the
tug. It was a wet. disagreeable Job,
but, then, what was it compared with
the weeks Martin and Costa have gone
without a dry stitch to their backs and

allon eggs?

ENGINEER AND
STATION AGENT
WERE AT FAULT
The Blame for the Newman Rail=
road Disaster Is Fixed
Upon Two Men,
R. B. ALLEN.
The engineer of the wrecked teachers' special, "upon whom blame is placed
for thi disaster.
OAKLAND, July U.â Engineer Allen and Conductor Fassett of the
Hl-fated train v.-recked at Newman yesterday returned to their homes
in this city to-night. They both feigned the strictest reticence when ap
proached by a reporter.
"I would not care to make any statement for a day or two." said
Allen. "I haven't been subpenaed to attend the inquest as yet, and
then will be time enough to talk. I would not lay the blame for the
wreck on any one, but I do feel there is no blame resting on myself or
our crew: I do not wish to state whether the lights at Newman indi
cated a clear track or not. I am sorry, but I will no.t give my version
of the affair until the proper time."
Conductor Fassett. when seen at his home, .863 Willow street, to
night, declined at first to speak, but finally volunteered a statement in
dicating that he regards the freight train crew responsible.
"I saw the lights at Newman just after the wreck and they were
for a clear track ahead, but of course lights may be wrong, just as peo
ple are sometimes wrong, and we are expected to use some judgment in
such matters. I would not place the blame. However, the freight train
was behind time, and it was my intention to hold my train at Newman
at least six minutes, because it is a rule that trains are not allowed to
follow each other within less than ten minutes, except in case special
permission is granted. I realized that we were nearing Newman sta
tion and I felt my train slacking down in speed. A minute later
there was a crash. Â®It was awful.
"I have not been subpenaed to the inquest yet. That is generally
done through the company. They attend to those matters. I can't feel
that our crew was to blame for the terrible wreck."
NEWMAN, July 11.â The bodies of Mrs. Lena Thomas and Miss Addie Harris,
who were killed in the train wreck here yesterday, were sent north on the
early morning train to-day. After the coffins were, placed in wooden boxes
a number of the ladies of this place called at Schank's undertaking parlors
and attached to' the rough boxes many beautiful floral offerings.
The wreckers have about finished clearing the wreckage from the .tracks and
placing it to one side to be hauled to the scrap-iron yards in this city.
The officials of the Southern Pacific Railroad have made a partial investiga
tion of the accident of Monday at Nswman, by which two women were killed and
many injured, and have fixed the blame upon Engineer Archie Ben Allen of the
teachers' excursion train and E. C. Hartwell, the station agent. With the latter it
was an error in the use of the semaphore signals, and with the former it was a
criminal blunder in running ahead of his time when he must have known that ha
was close to another train.
This is the judgment of J. A. Fillmore, manager of the Southern Pacific Rail
road Company's system. All day Monday the officials of the road were calling for
reports from their subordinates who were interested in or knew of the circum
stances attending the wreck. Mr. Fillmore, in discussing the sad- accident, .iesis
nated the freight as regular, No. 245. lie said the orders issued were that No. 'jls
should keep the main line and be passed when overtaken, and, according to cal
culations, this would occur at Ingomar. ten miles from where the wreck took
place The orders to the special excursion were that the train should keep out of
the way of all regular trains. By the signal system of the line this could havo
been done for at almost any one of the stations additional orders could be given
and received if there were any. According to the manager the regular. No. 245,
arrlvi '1 a.t Newman two minutes ahead of time, and the engineer, seeing by the
sigi al on the semaphore that there were no orders, the locomotive was stopped at
:ii. vater tank to take on a supply for the boilers.
When there are no orders the arm of the semaphore is down at an acute anjrla
and the white light is exposed. This was in condition when the regular, No. 245,
was standing at the tank, and the statijn agent could not put up the red light and
the extended arm while this train was there. When the special came In eight Sta
tion Agent E. C. Hartwell turned the semaphore to the red light, and upon En
gineer Allen whistling for signals Hartwell dropped the arm, showing the whita
light This was the- fatal mistake, and his excuse that he thought the excursion
would take the siding and pass the freight train seems to have but little weiyht at
headquarters. ' , .
The report of Engineer Allen of the special is that he was watching the
signals on the semaphore, for which he whistled, and did not drop his eyes to
the track ahead of him. Consequently he failed to see the lights on the rear
end of the regular freight until too late.
"Is there any question about the lights being on thÂ© rear of the freight
train?" was asked. . , .
"None whatever. The usual lights, were there all right, and, more than
that, tho brakeman, seeing the approaching train and fSaring that it was
likely to get too close, â¢grabbed the first lantern that he could getâ a white
lightâ and rushed down the track about 400 feet, waving it to attract the atten
tion of the engineer of the oncoming train," answered Mr. Fillmore, who went
on to say that the fault of Engineer Allen was that he had no business to have
been so near another train. The rules of the road require that a special
should be at least ten minutes behind regular trains.
"The blame for this sad accident," continued Mr. Fillmore, "lies with the en
gineer of the special and with the station operator. Allen was at fault for be
ing six minutes ahead of his time without having his engine under control.
That he reversed the lever and put on the air brakes to avert a collision when
he saw the lantern and the lights on the freight train is a fact, but that did not
lessen the damage. The station operator was at fault for giving a 'clear' sig
nal when the regular freight was standing on the main track at the station."
Mr. Fillmore said lhat the freight train was composed of about forty cars,
and, with the exception of the man on the extreme endâ he who ran down the
track with thf pignal lanternâ none of the crew knew of the seriousness of the
collisidn for several minutes. The engineer was first attracted to the occur
rence by a slight jar to the engine, for the train was so long that the shock
was lost before it reached the front end. To the engineer of the regular no
blame is attached by the railroad officials.
Engineer Allen has been suspended, but whethpr temporarily or permanent
ly is not knov â â¢. Tie has been running trains over that route for many years.
His first start in railroading was in 1888 as a fireman, and until the accident
of last Monday he has been rated as a careful and trustworthy employe.

Special Dispatch to The Call.
COVELO, July 11.â Excitement due
to the mysterious disappearance, of
! Hattie Waltham is at high tension here
I to-night. Scores of horsemen and
[ searching parties afoot are scouring
j the ranges, canyons and mountain
! sides for a trace of the missing girl.
' Panthers, bears and mountain lions
; abound in the neighboring thickets,
: and grave fears are entertained for her
j safety.
Miss Waltham, who has been known |
| as the "Rose of Round Valley." is the j
beautiful stepdaughter of Sylvester
Palmer, a wealthy stockraiser of the
J section. She is a fair flower of the
! mountains, much loved by the people
!of Round Valley. She was last seen at
5 o'clock last night, after which hour
jno trace of her has been found. The
young woman of late had showed indi
cations of depressed spirits and has
suffered greatly from fits of melan
choly. Her homo life was surrounded
i by every comfort, and no theory can be
advanced by her stepfather. Sylvester
| Palmer, as a probable cause for her
disappearance. Mrs. Palmer says Miss
Hattie was about the house as usual

JOHN W. GATES LIVES
Offers Aid to a Man Arrested for a
Murder of Twenty-eight
Years Ago.
MEXICO. Mo., July 11.â James Lee, an
inmate of the Missouri Penitentiary, Jef
ferson City. Mo., writes to a newspaper
here that Gilbert Gates, brother of John
W. Gates of Chicago, and for whose mur
der twenty-eight years afco Alexander
Jester is under arrest, was alive in 1894.
The letter In full follows:
I have noticed the publications In re
gard to the Jester case and will kindly
ask you if a deposition from me .vill
be given consideration, as I am well
acquainted with Gates and know that
he was alive and well in August. 1594,
and living in Arizona under the name
of George Dorsey. If you will write
to Monahan & Murphy, who run a
general store in Needles, Cal., they
will remember him. for we were min
ing and prospecting partners and often
bought supplies from them. If I can
be of service in the case I will give
you all the information I can. _ Jours
truly JAMES LEE.
Box 47. Convict No. 14.388.
Jester refused to-day to talk about the
convict's letter. He is growing stronger
every day and seems confident that he
will be cleared of the charge. He still
denies that he is the man wanted.

pino Leader.
BOISE, Idaho, July 11.â A. Mueller,
a wealthy brewer, has Just returned
from Manila. He was at the Philippines

sensational statement, quoting Dewey,

into harbor, in pursuance of a prear
ranged plan, the promises made Aguin
aldo coming from Washington. The

terness. Mueller is president of one of

MINNEAPOLIS. Minn., July 11.â A

ELECTIOTT AT BOISE.
Republicans Score an Overwhelming
Victory.
BOISE, Idaho, July 11.â The city elec
tion here resulted in a complete vic
tory for the Republicans. Judge J. H.
Richards, a former Coloradoan, was
elected Mayor, and six of the nine
Councilmen are Republicans. The Re
publicans also elected the Attorney,
Tax Collector and Treasurer, losing the
Clerk and the Assessor. Women Clerk
and Tax Collector were elected. Ex
cept on the head of the ticket the Re
-1 publican majority averages 250 out of
a total of 1563 votes cast. The vote was
nearly 1200 short as compared to that
of the last general election, there be
ing almost that many stay-at-homes.
In 1896 this city gave Bryan a ma
jority of 916 votes. The head of the
Republican ticket was slashed because
in 1896 Richards left the Republican
party, coming back last year. He re
ceived but thirty-five majority over his
Democratic opponent.

PATRICK KERVIN DIES
UNDER SURGEON'S KNIFE
Mining Expert Succumbs, to the
Effects of an Operation for
Chronic Pneumonia.
SAN JOSE, July 11.â Patrick Kervin,
one of the best-known mining men in
the West, died at the O'Connor Sani
tarium in this city at 7 o'clock this
evening of pneumonia. He came to the
sanitarium on June 13. Previous to
this he had been in the Waldeck Hos
pital at San Francisco for a month.
Kerwin was a native of County Wex
ford, Ireland. 61 years of age. He came
to California in ISR3 and engaged in
mining, which he had followed success
fully since. Kervin was superintendent
of the Consolidated Virginia, Best &
Belcher, Gould & Curry and Utah
mines. He was also interested in these
and other properties. For a number of
years he was superintendent of the On
tario at Park City, Utah. He returned
to California eleven years ago and be
came identified with the Comstock
mines. A widow and three children
survive him.
As Kervin gradually grew worse the
pneumonia became chronic and the
only hope of saving his life lay in an
operation, which was performed this
afternoon. He never rallied.
Kervin was a practical miner, and
his judgment was greatly sought by
corporations. He aided in developing
many mines and did much to advance
properties undm his superintends'!!' y.
He is reported to have had a consider
able fortune. Arrangements for the
funeral have not yet been made.

EDUCATIONAL POLICY
OF NEW POSSESSIONS
Ideas Suggested by United
States Commissioner of
Education Harris.
LOS ANGELES. July 11.â The evening
session was as largely attended as had I
been the one in the afternoon. In addi- j
tion to the Woman's Orchestra, the Aoe
dean Club, under the direction of Mrs.
G. B. Parsons, supervisor of music in the
public schools, enlivened the proceedings
by the very happy rendering of several
â¢ lons.
Dr. W. T. Harris, United States Com
missioner of Education, delivered a most
appropriate and decidedly interesting ad
dress on the subject of "An Educational
Policy for Our New Possessions." One
remarkable statement that apparently i
passed unheeded was that we took 11 for
granted that both Cuba and Porto Rico
would be under United States armed con
trol for several years. The States of
! modern Europe all seek to obtain a reve
! nue from their new possessions. This is
| all right so far as the ends of just gov- |
ernment are subserved. Up to a certain i
point there is an altruistic effort on the !
I part of these modern nations to uplift !
these new peoples. From the United ;
States one looks for a new step more al
truistic. It is a strong dash of this Idea
that has created the party of anti-expan
sion. They believe that none should be
admitted to the rights of citizenship who
are unable to govern themselves. The
admission of peoples unable to be ad
mitted to citizenship seems to be a de
parture and to threaten some of our
cherished Institutions. Expansion in
some form is seen, however, to be un
avoidable. In view of the fact that other
nations are dividing up the world, some
people think it our duty to show what
we can do. In concluding, he said:
The United States can uplift whole nations.
Is not this better than to build a Chinese wall
about our liberties? It Is absurd to say that
we will tyrannize over those incapable of self
government. We must accept the charge of such
colonies as come to us and give them the bene
fits-of civilization. But it lÂ£ asked what right
have we to give other people our civilization
by force. In other words, is ours a high civil
ization and what is the highest form? A people
is civilized when it has formed institutions
which enable each Individual to benefit and
prosper by the thoughts and deeds of all his
fellows and In turn benefit them. Civilization
enables man to conquer nature and make it his
servant. There is no tribal civilization on the
face of the earth an.l never was that compares
with modern nations. A nation may be very
far advanced in its control of the forces of
nature and get the benefit of past experience
and yet give those benefits only to the few. The ,
highest Ideal of a civilization is that which
is constantly lifting lower peoples to a higher ;
plane and Increasing the individual effective
ness and that cannot be paid to exist in a
tribal form or even in some far advanced na- !
tionalities. If we cannot come into contact ,
with lower nations without exterminating them I
we are still far from the goal. We must take

to some extent by giving an address re- i

in the islands. Owing to her unavoidable I

made a thoughtfully conceived address
upon the subject of "The Educational
Problem in Hawaii." He said that minds
in general develop In accordance with cer
tain fixed and ascertainable laws, yet the
first principle's of the science imply that
no two men think, or feel, or will, or de
velop alike, since no two have the same
temper and environment. 1 In like man
ner, he continued, it follows that different
races and peoples develop differently and
hence have their own educational prob
lems, which are but modifications of tho
world's great educational problem. Con
cluding Dr. Townsend said:
The environment nnd the temper of th*
children of Hawaii are especially diversified,
although this environment of things Is com-
Varatively homogeneous. This Is due largely
to the diversity of races, with all that that im
plies. The ends of the earth meet at Hawaii,
and no one race is predominant In numbers.
Present indications are that the generation
next to be born in Hawaii will consist of white
persons and Hawaiians In almost equal num
bers, and of still large numbers of Asiatics.
These latter will not be assimilated with the
other elements of our future population so as
to form one people, though It is possible that
the whites and the Hawaiians will thus unite.
In spite of radical racial differences. One prob
lem involves at least two races of men In
nearly equal numbers living side by side within
our narrow boundaries without uniting. Poly
nesians and Asiatics cannot be made to think
and feel as Anglo-Saxons by the simple process
of teaching them the English language, or by
any other process which does not Involve evolu
tion through nations. Yet, in the meantime,
we must live together. "What, then. Is this
great race Droblem In Hawaii? It is this: To
develop a truly Christian civilization, a civ
ilization which like the love of Christ Is broad
enough to embrace all peoplesâ so broad that all
the races may find prosperty under its benign
sway. Our Hawaiian civilization should be
based upon broad charity and universal good
will.
The civilization which the active race haÂ«
developed and in which it has been trained,
based upon an assumption of universal selfish
ness, has precipitated a struggle in which the
passive race is at a great disadvantage. It is
a melancholy fact that the influence of the
white man and his civilization In contact with
the aboriginal races is generally the Influence
of the fable of the upas tree. From the lime
of the ancient Hebrews, who shut from the
blessings of their civilization practically all
other peoples, down to the time of the Amer
icans, who have not yet developed a civiliza
tion sufficiently broad and sufficiently Chris
tian to bless the white man and the red, cornea
the same monotonous story. Here the native
Hawaiian* were born and here they will die.
They call for more than toleration. Who are
we that we should tolerate this remnant of the
people to Whom God gave those fair isles fresh
from his creative hand and who for nearly
fifty generations have known no other home?

PAID DEARLY FOR
THEIR LEGAL STRIFE
MILL. VALLEY, July 11.â In a de
cision handed down by the Supreme
Court of the State last Thursday is
written the end of a famous contro
versy between two wealthy residents of
this place, which has been fought cut
in the Superior Courts for a period of
nearly fifteen years. Thousands of dol
lars have been expended by both liti
gants and long ago the expense of mak
ing the legal battle footed up a great
deal more than the value of the prop
erty which was the bone of contention,
but both men were too stubborn to
yield.
The two litigants are Jacob Garde
ner and Ebenezer Wormouth. Years
ago the trouble between them origi
nated over the ownership of forty acres
of land, valued now at about $1000 an
acre. They went to law and for six or
seven years the case was pending in
the Superior Court of Marin County
and then passed on to the Government
Land Office. The Supreme Court has
finally decided in favor of Wormouth.
The victory proves a costly one, as
between the two men about $50,000 is
computed to have been spent in liti
gation. One man alone made $1000 sim
ply in publishing the legal notices re
quired by the case. Both men are cele
brated characters. It was about two
years ago that Wormouth was sand
bagged and robbed at night, and last
summer while he was absent in Sausa
lito his house was looted by a burglaj-.
Gardener recently had a disagreement
over the ownership of a piece of land
with another citizen, which was settled
by his opponent building a shanty on
the land and holding it with a shotgun.
FLEES TO AVOID
PAYING ALIMONY
SAN JOSE, July 11.â Henry M. N.
Spring, Councilman and clothier, has
disposed of his interest in the firm of
T. W. Spring & Son and gone to
Chicago, presumably to avoid paying
his former wife, Mrs. Emilie Spring,
$200 a month alimony. A few weeks
ago Mrs. Spring obtained a divorce
from her husband on the gTound of in
fidelity, two San Francisco young
ladies being named as corespondents.
She was awarded the custody of three
minor children and given $200 a month
alimony.
Spring went on the witness-stand and
said he was penniless. This was a sur
prise, because his interest in the cloth
ing house of Spring & Son was esti
mated to be worth about $40,000, and,
besides, he had other property.
TOday bill of sale was JJle<J, J>y
which Spring transferred one-half In
terest in the clothing house to Fred "W.
Moore, his brother-in-law, the con
sideration being given as $8000. At the
same time two deeds were filed trans
ferring his fine residence and the ad
joining lot at Seventh and San An
tonio streets to Marcella S. Moore, his
sister. It states the transfer is made
for "love and affection" and "also for
better maintenance, support, protection
and livelihood of the grantor."
Spring represents the Third Ward in
the City Council. It is said his resigna
tion is now in the hands of Mayor
Martin.

GRAND DUKE MICHAEL
TO BE THE SUCCESSOR
Czar Nicholas of Russia Names His
Brother as Heir to the
Throne.
ST. PETERSBURG, July 11.â The Of
ficial Messenger to-day publishes an im
perial manifesto worded as follows:
"Our beloved brother and heir to the
throne, the Grand Duke George Alex
androvitch, died at Abb;\s Tuman,
June 28 (old style). The illness which
attacked him might, it was hoped,
yield to the treatment initiated and the
intluence of the southern climate, but
God willed otherwise. In submitting
without a murmur to the decree of
Providence, we call our faithful sub
jects to share our deep sorrow with us
and to offer fervent prayers for the re
pose of the soul of our departed
brother.
"Henceforward, and so long as it may
not please God to bless us with a son,
the right of succession to the throne
devolves, according to the precise defi
nition of the law of succession, upon
our beloved brother, the Grand Duke
Michael Alexandrovitch.
"NICHOLAS."
Miners Will Get Their Pay.
SAX ANDREAS, July 11.â As was pre
dicted in Saturday's dispatches to The
Call, the late difficulties of the Fort
Wayne Gold Producing Company of In
diana arc about at an end. Ten days ago
the works and cash in bank at Stockton
wore attached by the striking workmen,
who ha<] two months' wages due them.
This was owing; to the company not pro
ducing on pay day, a-^ some difficulties
existed between the local management
and the Eastern stockholders. J. F.
Clapp, manager and promoter of the
property for many yearn, will be super-
ceded. The miners who attached the
property have agreed to pay costs if the
stockholders pay back wages for two
months. This was mutual, and a perfect
understanding seems to exist. Work will
be resumed at an early date.
WIDESPREAD PLOT
TO REMOVE MILAN
NEW YORK. July 11.â The Sun's cable
from Vienna says: The Politlsche corre
spondent says that an inquiry into the
recent attempt to assassinate ex-King
Milan of Servia shows that it was a part
of an astonishingly widespread conspiracy
and was intended as a prologue to the
overthrow of the dynasty and the entire
present regime.
Other reports indicate that the persons
arrested in connection with the attempt
include three ex-Minlsters. three ex-Sec
retaries of State, two Judges of the Court
of Appeals, nine members of the Skupsh
tina, or parliamentary body, nine pro
fessors and a sprinkling of military and
police officers, priests, students and civil
servants.

NO RELIEF YET FOR
THE FIRE DEPARTMENT
The Finance Committee of the Board
of Supervisors met last evening and failed
to do anything more than preserve tha
status quo of the pretty little game ol
politics just now being played by the Firs
Department. Representatives of nearly
every improvement club in "the city were
on hand to speak either for or against an
increase in the department's allowance in
the tax levy, and there was a good bit ol
mighty spell-binding done. The commit
tee, however, suddenly broke off Its de
liberations and adjourned to this after
noon at 2 o'clock without taking action.
Fire Commissioner Bohen was heard
first. He merely said he had come to get
an answer from the committee to the
j propositions that he had made. Chairman
: Perrault replied that the committee had
j thought that it could take a portion ol
the amount asked for by the department
i out of the police fund, but had discovered
later that it could not. "The only other
I thing we can do," continued Mr. Perrault,
"is to take $9000 out of the urgent neces
sity fund."
Chief Sullivan got up at this and said
that $!K)00 would carry the department
over the month of July very nicely. Asked
by Perrault if the amount was to be used
for salaries, he replied that it was not,
that the salaries were provided for and
that the $9000 would be used for fodder
and the general expenses of the depart
ment.
A parley among: the members of the
committee ensued at Perrault's d^sk and
he subsequently announced that some of
the members were not In favor of ex
tending even the temporary relief he had
suggested, and that the meeting would
have to go over for a day or so. Al
though Sullivan had expected a decision,
and so had the half dozen heads of im
provement clubs who had been got into
line, be made no objection to postpone
ment, and it was so ordered. The ques
tion will come up again this afternoon at
2 o'clo; k.
Chief of Police I,ecs was called upon to
show cause why his department allow
ance should not be slashed, and he did so
by informing the board that most of his
apportionment was provided for by stat
ute and could not be cut. He also pointed
out that under the new charter the park
police would be made a part of his de
partment and incidentally he took occa
sion t<> say that tlie general expense of
government would bp greatly increased
by the provisions of the charter.
Colin M. Boyd appeared to protest
against any reduction in the public
library fund, and Treasurer Truman and
Joe Goddard, chief deputy under County
Cl^rk D.-ane, came In for the same pur
pose. Dr. Barbat appeared for the Board
of Hf-alth and waved the yellow flag of
quarantine wildly In an attempt to con
vince the committee that his department
would need iKiT.ISu to pull through the
year with. He held up smallpox and the
possibility of an epidemic of It as a very
strong probability ami wound up by read
ing the rather extensive list of employes
to whom the hoard is now paying salaries.
After bearing rt pr< sentatives of differ
ent improvement clubs in protests against
curtailing the Fire Department, adjourn
ment was taken to this afternoon.
DEATH REMOVES A
PIONEER MERCHANT
Etienne Gulttard. a pioneer chocolata
manufacturer of the Pacific Coast, suf
fered a stroke of apoplexy last Monday
while In Dr. Burkes sanitarium at Santa
Rosa and passed away a few minutes aft
erward. The deceased was known
throughout the West and the news of his
death will be received with regret by all
who enjoyed his friendship. He came to
this State in IS6S and established a coffee
and chocolate business. His fame as a
business man was widespread and he en-
Joyed a lucrative income from his splen
did Investment.
He was a generous man and many poor
families owe their relief from starvation
to his kindness. During- the critical
period in Monterey County recently, when
the residents in that vicinity were suffer
ing on account of the drought, Mr. Guit
tard quietly and unostentatiously spent
his money and brought sunshine to the
homes of many needy ranchers.
The deceased was prominent In Masonic
circles and up to the time of his illness
took an active part in the order. Ho was
also a member of the French Benevolent
Society. Merchants' Association and kin
dred organizations.
Mr. Guittard was 61 years of age at the
time of his demise. He leaves a widow
and eight children to mourn his loss. The
latter are George and Horace Gulttard.
Mrs. Cavalsky and Emil, Adrlenne, Frank
and Beatrice Guittard. The funeral will
be held to-morrow at 12:30 o'clock from
his late residence. 1825 Clay street, thence
to Masonic Temple, where services will
be held under the auspices of La Parfait
Union No. 17. F. and A. M. The inter
ment will be in Masonic Cemetery.
CALIFORNIA WILL GET
ONE-HALF THE SPACE
The Paris Commission yesterday re
ceived a letter from G. B. Brockett, head
of the division of pomology in the United
States Department of Agriculture at
Washington. D. C, notifying the Com
missioners of the allotment of space that
had been made for the display of canned
and evaporated goods from California.
The space given to this State, though
circumscribed. Is one-half of the total
space allowed the United States. The
area of the compartment, however, Is
quite sufficient to allow the best of our
products in this line, and none but the
best will be accepted, to receive ample
notice. It is 15 feet in length, 6 feet in
depth and 10 feet in height.
There has been no space given for the
display of fresh fruit preserved in glass
in its natural state, but in the cases of
a few exceptionally fine specimens room
will be made somewhere.

STRANDED LINER PARIS
HAULED OFF THE ROCKS
Removed From Her Critical
Position and Will Be Towed
to Falmouth.
LONDON, July 11.â The salvagers have moved the American line
steamer Paris astern for a distance of 150 yards and have
shifted the vessel's position slightly to the eastward. They
hope to be able to get the after stoke hole fires alight.
The Paris is now clear of the rocks. The sea la smooth,
but a heavy fog prevails to-night. The intention of the salvagers was
simply to slue the stern of the liner so as to facilitate the operations
of the divers, but it was found that she moved more freely than was
expected. Three salvage boats alone practically removed her from a
critical position unaided. She still has a distinct list to starboard and
cannot be assumed out of danger. The tugs are preparing to tow her to
Falmouth harbor.
The German salvagers are elated at their unexpected success in
floating the Paris. She had been weighted with nearly a thousand tons
of granite at the stern, with a view of hoisting her bows free from
the rock. The divers had difficulty in getting at the rock, and this
was the reason for the tentative effort to move her. Several tugs from
Falmouth proceeded to the scene, but the Germans were not anxious
their assistance, offering the largest tug only $25 for towage, an offer
which was promptly refused. It was then decided that the vessel, be
ing in a position of comparative safety, should remain where she was
for the night and that the divers should continue their work of patch
ing the hull, so as to minimize the risk of towing. Unless something
unforeseen occurs, the Paris will be towed to Falmouth to-morrow, and
if on inspection her condition warrants it she will be taken to South
ampton or to some other dock for repairs.
The weather late this evening is rather unfavorable, the symptoms
being rain, fog, a falling barometer and a slight wind from the south
east. An increase in the wind might prove serious. Every precaution
has been taken to anchor the liner securely. The coast guards are
keeping a close lookout.
Captain Watkins, the other officers and twenty members of the
crew are still on board. The pumps are coping with the water and
there is no danger of the vessel sinking. It is understood that the sal
vagers are the same parties who floated the Peninsular and Oriental
steamer China, which went ashore in March last year on Azadea point.

FED BY CHARITY
Active Workof the Texas j
Relief Committees.
SIX CONVICTS DROWNED
I CONDITION OF AFFAIRS IN TWO
INUNDATED COUNTIES.
i Cotton Speculators in East I'ake Ad
vantage of Loss of Crops, but Gov
ernor Sayers Will Not Enter
a Gambling Controversy.
Special Dispatrh to The Call.
AUSTIN, Tex., July 11.â Not less
than 20,000 hungry men. women and
! children in the flooded districts of
Texas were fed by the bountiful hand
'' of charity to-day. It is probable that
there were a few thousand sufferers in
the remote districts of the blighted re
gion who did not receive their quota
of food, but this was through no fault
of the many committees who have ac
tive charge of the relief and distribut
ing work.
The worst condition of affairs in the
; inundated region now exists in Brazo
-1 ria and Fort Btnd counties, and it will
I probably be several days before there
Is much improvement in that section.
Six State penitentiary convicts lost
their lives in Fort Bend County yes
terday by drowning. The penitentiary
! board of directors will meet here to
i morrow to take action in regard to tne
heavy LOSS Of crops and property on
! the convict plantations in Fort Bend
County, which are still under water.
Governor Sayers received a telegram
to-day from W. Gerard of Baltimore,
i Md., stating that the extent of the
: damage to crops, and the Buffering ol
the people is being belittled for cold
blooded mercenary reasons, and that
the New York cotton speculators are
heavily short on the market for that
reason. The telegram concludes by ask
-1 ing the Governor to discredit the re
! ports and let the world at large knew
the true condition of affairs. The Gov
ernor replied by wiring Mr. Gerard that
'< he would not allow himself to become
involved with the gamblers in cotton
on the one side or the other. The Gov
ernor does not hesitate to say, however,
; that the loss to the cotton crop is as
great as has been estimated in any of
the published reports. The loss will
considerably exceed 500,000 bales.
S. Taliaferro, chairman of the Harris
County relief committee, wired Gover
nor Sayers to-day as follows:
"Our advices show enough supplies of
! bacon, flour and corn meal at points
'. from Hempstead down to supply ac
tual necessities to points east of the
river for about three days. There is a
I congestion at some points and a mea
: ger supply at others. I have sent men
i out on the line of the railway to rem
! edy this. Our goods are very low. Col
â¢ leeting committees are still out. We
i are having negrroes held at distribut
ing places so they can go to work when
the water recedes, but they will have
; to be fed and assisted when they re
turn to work as well as while at dis
tributing points."
It is estimated that over 100 carloads
of food supplies and clothing were con
tributed by the people of the State for
the sufferers to-day. A number of
large cash contributions were received,
; among them being $10,000 from the C.
P. Simmons Medicine Company of St.
, Louis, through their Texas customers.
ASTORIA'S GREETING TO
OREGON VOLUNTEERS
Big Marine Procession to Meet the
Transports Newport and
Ohio.
ASTORIA, Or., July 11.â The several
patriotic societies of this city are mak
ing extensive preparations to welcome
the returning Oregon volunteers upon
their arrival here.
To-night Mayor Bergman wired to
General Summers, who is returning
with the troops, asking him to allow
the boys to land here for five or more
hours, the intention being to banquet
them. The Mayor also wired Governor
(Jeer asking for his co-operation. It is
the purpose of the people of this city to
get up a monster marine procession and
meet the Newport and Ohio at the
heads. The troops will be brought
ashore and banqueted and otherwise
entertained if General Summers' con
sent to the arrangements can be ob
tained. Without doubt the request will
be granted, as several hoursâ perhaps
a dayâ will be required to transfer the
troops from the transports to the river
steamers.
The coming of the transports will be
signaled to Astoria from Tillamook
Rock, and the marine procession will
immediately leave to meet them.

.ment.

he had better stick to the story he told

tively identify the defendant. Clark
said that he had not been approached

fendant, but now he doubted that he
had seen the defendant on the day of I
the riot because of the facts which had
come to his knowledge since that time.
Manager Burbidge of the Bunker Hill

as did Superintendent Burch, who was
under cross-examination when court
adjourned.

Ice Plant for Marysville.
MARYSVILLE, July 11.â A New York !
capitalist is in this city with a view to :
selecting a location for an icemaking ma
chine and cold-storage plant, and from
his observations he has concluded that
Marysville presents a good opening for
such an undertaking. He represents a
company to be incorporated with the
name of the locality selected. The town
will be required to make a donation of
the site, situated so that a switch may be
run from the railroad. It is proposed to
build a ten-ton ice plant, the product to
be sold at not to exceed three-quarters of
a cent a pound. The cold-storage build
ing is to be 50x300 feet, the entire cost of
the plant to be $30,000. A meeting of the
improvement association has been called
for next Tuesday evening to discuss the
advisability of giving the bonus. ..

Special Dispatch to The Call.
CONCORD, N. H., July 11.âRepresenta

paign in this State. The specific charge
is that Senator Gallinger, as chairman of

uted circulars appealing to Republican
office-holders for contributions to the
campaign funds.
Civil Service Commissioners J. B. Har
low and Mark S. Brewer, who arrived

Interested, though whether as a prose

of the Department of Justice will be
called on.
At the conclusion of the morning ses

draw his own conclusions.

DAWSONITES GLAD
TO FLOCK TO THE
NEW GOLD FIELDS
Tactics of the Dominion in Re=
serving the Best Claims Drive
Many to Cape Nome.
By SAM W. WALL.
Special Correspondence of The Call.
DAWSON, N. W. T., June 20.â Reports
of rich findings of gold at Cape Nome,
near the mouth of the Yukon, which
reached here only a few days ago, are
attracting great numbers of prospectors
from this place and from all along the
river. Readier credence is given the story
than would be the case were not the
hitherto unsuccessful prospectors so
.thoroughly disgusted with the shifting,
uncertain but always burdensome regu
lations that govern here. It is of course
the unsuccessful prospectors who are
leaving, in the main, but many also who
have met with a considerable measure
of success here are joining the stampede.
It may be said that news that might be
absolutely relied upon reporting rich dig
gings on the American side would almost
depopulate this city and these diggings
so entirely disheartened are the long
suffering and patient miners here. The
peremptory withdrawal of El Dorado and
Bonanza creeks from location by the
public has brought an armj' of prospect
ors to town, who have been employed
along the hillsides and in the pups and
gulches of those two creeks. They would
have remained digging and searching
along the margins of these rich pay
streaks all summer, but, almost to a man,
these people have bought tickets for
down the river. The town and the entire
diggings, especially the diggings, the
backbone of the whole thing, are in a
state of nervous prostration resulting
from the reports that follow one another
in quick succession and which gain ready
credence since this remarkable order, at
first laughed at as Impossible of belief,
has been certified by the authorities as
a fact. It is reported that other creeks
are about to be closed and the latest is
that after a certain date no more miners'
licenses or renewals of claims will be
issued. The authorities here, being ques
tioned, can only say that they know of
no such proposed changes, but inasmucn
as they did not know, or say they did not
know, of the order closing El Dorado and
Bonanza until they tore open the en
velope in which the order was contained,
this is not satisfying. The demoralizing
effect on the camp can hardly be overes
timated. . , m ..
What substance there is back of the
story that comes from St. Michael or
Cape Nome I do not know. The geneial
disposition is to charge it to the trans
portation companies, which are aheauy
benefiting so largely by it. Three steam
ers that have gone down the river since
transportation opened were crowded. Tho
up-river traffic has opened disastrously.
The James Donvllle. under charter at
Captain Sid Barrington, was wrecked in
Thirty-Mile River and, except the ma
chinery, is a total loss. The Columbian
of the* Canadian Development Company
GATHERING DATA
REGARDING TRADE
Will Show Necessity for
New Department.
WORK AHEAD FOR CONSULS
MUST REPORT ON COMMERCE
AND INDUSTRIES.
Wide Scope of the Information De
sired Shows the Vast Amount
of Business the New Cabi
net Office Will Handle.
Special Dispatch to The Call.
CALL HEADQUARTERS. WEL
LINGTON HOTEL, WASHINGTON,
! July 11.â Action has been taken by the
State Department looking to the collec
tion of Important data respecting the
trade of the United States with foreign
countries and of such countries with
each other, which will be presented to
Congress about January 1.
This information will be used to show
the necessity of establishing a depart
ment not only for the administration of
colonial affairs, but for the protection
and extension of American commerce,
to be known as the Department of Co
lonial Affairs and Foreign Commerce.
Assistant Secretary Cridler has sent
to consular officers instructions to pre
pare and forward to the department
not later than November 1 a report on
the commerce and industries of each
consular district, covering any facts
and figures for the yeaj 1898 not al
ready transmitted, and as complete and
accurate a statement as may be ob
tained of trade and industries of the
district for the six months ending June
30. 1899.
The wide scope of the Information de
sired shows the vast amount of busi
ness which a department such as is
now regarded as necessary would have
to handle. Information Is asked for,
among other things, as to the Increase
or decrease in the more Important lines
of exports and importÃ, especially such
as enter into the trade of the United
States, conditions of transportation,
! exchange, banking, etc., tariff rates
and customs rules, port regulations,
! wharfage dues, improvements in har-
b or facilities, extension of telegraph
i and cable service, freight rates, regula
tions affecting commercial travelers,
conditions of the merchant marine,
regulations as to quarantine and laws
or regulations of a discriminating
character which affect American ves
sels and trade.

ARMY TRANSPORTS
WILL NOW BE ARMED
NEW YORK. July 11.â A special to the
Sun from Washington says: The Navy
Department has consented to turn over
to the Ordnance Bureau of the army six
teen six-pounder rifles for use on army
transports engaged in carrying troops to
and from the Philippines. The troop
ships have been "entirely without arma
ment.
It was said to-day by army officers that
if the Filininos had been enterprising
enough to mount on a ship some of the
guns they took from the Spanish gunboats
at Zaboango they might have captured
or destroyed some of the army transports.
One officer hinted that word had been re
ceived that the Filipinos were arming
some vessels and that this was the real
reason why the army transports were to
havo modern rifles.
The guns are at the Brooklyn Navy
yard, and will be shipped immediately
to the Washington ordnance factory for
some slight alterations before being in
stalled on board the transports at San
Francisco.

SURRENDERS TO WEST
VIRGINIA'S GOVERNOR
CHICAGO, July 11.â A special to the
Record from Louisville, Ky., says:
Ellas Hatfleld, the noted desperado and
son of "Devil Anse" Hatfield, notorious
in the Hatfleld-McCoy feud that cost
141 lives, surrendered to Governor At
kinson of West Virginia in person to
day at Gray. Hatfleld shot Deputy
Sheriff Ellis a week ago and has been
holding off a posse in the Kentucky
mountains. He sent word that he
would surrender to Governor Atkinson
only.

CONCERT FOR VETERANS.
Musical Selections Rendered in the
Camp Reynolds Pavilion.
SANTA CRUZ, July 11.â The Civil War
veterans are enjoying every moment of
the camp life at Camp Wallace Reynolds,
and the population of this tented city is
constantly on the increase. Only a few
more days remain before camp breaks and
all wiH be loth to leave their temporary
home by the seashore.
Last evening the assembly tent was so
crowded that half of those present were
compelled to stand. The programme was
In charge of Professor George Hastings.
It was of a patriotic nature and found a
ready response from the old soldiers, each
participant being encored with rounds of
applause. There were selections by the
Hastings orchestra; a vocal solo. "The
Rollcall," by Mrs. Fred D. McPherson:
a basÃ solo, "Freedom's Flag."' Charles
Fitch; solo, "After the Din of Battle,"
Miss Maud Hohman; solo, "The Happy
Days of Old," Mrs. J. R. Williamson. The
programme closed with Sousa's "Th'j
Stars and Stripes Forever," by Mrs. J.
R. Williamson, Mrs. F. D. McPherson,
Misses Nixon, Grace de Lamater, Anna
Linscott, Maud Hohman and Kate
Bantee.
SECRETARY ALGER
IS YET DEFIANT
Needs More Than Hints
to Retire.
IGNORES OFFICIAL SNUBS
DENIES THAT HE HAS BEEN
ASKED TO KESIGN.
General Francis V. Greene of Hew
York Mentioned as a Probable
Successor to the Head of
the War Department.
Special Dispatch to The Call.
CALX. HEADQUARTERS, WELL
INGTON HOTEL, WASHINGTON.
July 11.â A member of the Cabinet said
to-night that Secretary Alger will be
probably given time to finish his an
nual reports, and that if he did not
voluntarily retire by that time, his res
ignation would be asked for.
However, Secretary Alger wants
something more than hints and official
snubs to induce him to give up the
War Department portfolio. He will re
fuse to consider many instances of of
ficial ostracism, so apparent to every
body, as an intimation that the Presi
dent desires him to volunteer his resig
nation, and he told a friend again to
day that he would not resign until he
had a direct statement from the Presi
dent that such action on his part would
be desirable. Under such circum
stances he said, he would not of course
refuse to resign, declaring that like
other Cabinet officers, his resignation
was always at the disposal of the Presi-
Concerning the recent situation in re
gard to his retirement, the Secretary
refused to make any public statement
beyond this one brief sentence: "I deny
emphatically the report that I have re
fused to resign or that the President
has intimated to me that he wants me
to resign."
It is apparent from this that the Sec
retary does not yet fully appreciate
what all of his colleagues in the Cabi
net do, and that is that the President
would gladly welcome his resignation.
He persists in declaring that some of
his enemies in the Cabinet are at thÂ»
bottom of all the reports circulated
about him. There is no doubt that
pressure to get the Secretary out of
the Cabinet will continue, and it is dif
ficult to see how the Secretary can
stand it much longer.
NEW YORK, July 11.â General Fran
cis V. Greene, according to a report
current here, may be asked by Presi
dent McKinley to succeed General Al
ger as Secretary of War. General
Greene conferred with Governor Roose
velt and Senator Platt to-day, but it
could not be learned whether his possi
ble appointment was discussed.
It is expected that the Secretaryship
will be given to New York, which has
had no representation in the Cabinet
since Secretary Bliss resigned.

SON OF CAPITALIST
JOHN HERD INSANE
Sought to Kill Persons for Whom He
Conceived a Violent
Dislike.
STOCKTON. July 11.â John Herd Jr.,
son of John Herd, the capitalist who is
heavily interested in reclaiming projects,
is confined at Clark's Hospital here under
treatment for a mental trouble. Young-
Herd has been causing a vast amount of
trouble of late at Clifton Court, on Vic
toria Island, which is the headquarters
of the Old River Land and Reclamation
Company, of which hi? father is presi
dent. On Saturday night it was reported
a pitched battle was in progress between
him and some employes of the place, for
several of whom he had conceived a vio
lent hatred. Officers were sent to the
scene from both Martinez and Stockton,
I,ast week Herd crossed the river into
Contra Costa County and, armed with
a pistol, threatened to kill James Oliver,
the foreman. Trouble was averted for a
short time. He then went to the other
Fide and barricaded himself in a cabin.
locking all entrances, and nothing- could
be done with him.
The climax of the trouble was appar
ently reached when Patrick McKinney.
who rents land on the river, went on a
FpreÂ». Fie armed himself with a shotgun
and swore to kill young Herd. He walked
up and down the levee for five or six
hour?, shooting Into Herd's cabin at in
tervals. The distance was too far to per
mit- ol his doing- any damage, however,
but had McKinney had a rifle he prob
ably would have killed Herd.
The trouble apparently arose over the
ferry at Clifton Conrt. Young Herd, who
had charge of it, refused to carry certain
people for whom he had no liking.

BOG POISONER AT WORK.
Likely to Be Tarred and. Feathered
Should He Bs Caught.
TIBT'RON. July 11.â From the terry "slip
to the extreme end of Belvedere- penin
sula a trail has been "blazed." with dead
dogs as the landmarks. These mute wit
nesses to the presence of tn f - poisoner
around are fifteen in number and embrace
a variety of canine breeds, from the val
uable bird dog to the fluffy spitz and the

heart. If the poisoner is discovered he

were several very valuable animals. A
purse has been subscribed by the owners
of the poisoned dogs and a detective sum
mon"'! fr..m San Francisco to undertake
the task of discovering the guilty person.

INSPECTING SAN
JOAQUIN VALLEY
I Agricultural Commis
sion at Fresno.
SHOWN POINTS OF INTEREST
ONE IMPORTANT RESULT OF
THEIR VISIT.
Elwood Meade May Be Sent by the
Government to Investigate
California's Irriga
tion System.
Special Dispatch to The Call.
FRESNO, July 11.â As a result of the
visit of the Association of American
Agricultural Colleges and experiment
stations to this place there may be
brought about In California a complete
revision of the present irrigation sys
tem. Elwood Meade of the experiment
station. United States Department of
Agriculture, now stationed at Chey
enne, Wyo., is a Government expert on
irrigation. A committee of prominent
citizens of Fresno is considering the .
feasibility of getting the Government
to detail Meade to make a thorough in
vestigation of the State with a view to
having him make recommendations as
to a systematic improvement in the
present irrigation system, and also as
to whether the irrigation should be
under private or State ownership. The
matter is being conservatively discuss
ed by the citizens' committee, and it is
understood Meade will make his in
vestigation without any preconceived
ideas and report to the Government the
result. Mr. Meade says:
"In event of my being detailed to do
this work. I shall make an impartial
and disinterested investigation into the
conditions of the present irrigation sys
tem with a view to making recom
i mendations for a conservation and eco
i nomieal distribution of the water sup
ply, which I understand is not had at
present."
The private train of seven Pullmans
i having on board the delegates arrived
i here shortly after 8 o'clock this morn-
I ing. The visitors were met by Presi
dent Albert Lachman of the Califor
nia Winegrowers' Association and the
following committee of citizens: S. F.
Booth, Arthur R. Briggs. O. J. Wood
' ward. J. M. Collier, H. C. B. Gill. J. C.
Moore, Alexander Gordon and T. C.
White. The delegates were taken to
the Hotel Hughes for breakfast, after
I which they were taken aboard another
| special train to the San Joaquin ice
factory and creamery. A short stay
was next made at Las Palmas, after
which the visitors inspected the Malt
ermoro Company's St. George vine
yards, where they partook of light re
freshments.
A second special train conveyed the
delegates to the Caiwa Winery, where
{ they were tendered a luncheon by the
: California Wine Association. Presi- j
! dent J. E. Stubbs of the Agricultural
Association presided. He introduced
: < 'harles S. Murkiand of New Hamp
j shire, who thanked the citizens of
I Fresno for their hospitality and who
j moved a vote of thanks, which was
! unanimously adopted. William Han
i ford of the California Wine Associa
tion responded to th^ vote of thanks.
, Arthur R. Briggs on the part of the
| citizens' committee also expressed the
pleasure it had given the people of
Fresno to entertain the association.
In the evening a third excursion was
made to the Minnewawa Vineyard and
Kearney Ranch, which were inspected |
\ with gr^at interest by the visitors, j
The members of the association have \
been the guests of the Chamber of
Commerce, which is bearing the ex
pense of their entertainment here. The
delegates are praising California hos
pitality in the highest terms. F. S.
Scribner. agrastologist of the Depart
ment of Agriculture, Washington,
D. C. says:
"California, from all I have seen, \s
j the empiie State of the T'nion. It is a
i principality that could flourish inde
pendently ol the rest of the world.
Her*> you have everything in the
world."
The delegates remained at Fresno
until 10 o'clock to-night. \vh"n they left
for Â« Moville. which they will reach !
early to-morrow morning. Marys ville
and Woodland also will be visited to
morrow.

TRYING TO CONNECT MRS.
STOfiKE WITH MURDERS
Santa Barbara Authorities May Ac
cuss Her of the Killing- of the
Richardsons.
SANTA BARBARA, July 11.â The Santa
Barbara Authorities am about to take !
Btepfl to fasten the murrler of the Rieh
ardsons. three yeara apo. upon Mrs. Yda
Addis Storke, who yesterday was sen
tenced tQ San Quentin upon conviction nf
having libeled Dr. Windiest- r. Mrs.
Richardson and her daughter Ethel were !
murdered at their beautiful Mont eel to
home, ("ynis Bnrnard, an* employe at the
place, was killed while resisting arrest as
a suspect. The authorities believed at
that time that with his death the murder
was avenged. Now they are of the belief
thai Mrs. Storke planned the murder and
Barnard was her accomplice.
At the time of the discovery of Miss
Richardson's body In a field, on the morn- :
inp following the crime. Mrs. Storke was
almost thfv first person on the scene. She
declared she was there in <he role of a
detective and that she had been sent by
a citizen named Ivison. The latter de
clares that he never talked with her re
garding the crime, and that at the time
she appeared at the Richardson place he
did not know the murder had been com
mitted. The. recent attack by Mrs. Storke
upon tJrant Jackson is regarded as evi
dence that the woman is the victim of a
murderous mania.
More Union Bank Litigation.
SAN JOSE, July ILâ Suit was brought
to-day by H. A. Marckres for himself and
on behalf of all the depositors of the
J'nion Savings Bank to en.ioin the direct
ors and secretary of the bank from selling
the delinquent *tock on which assessment
lias been levied and is unpaid, advertised
for next Saturday. This sale, by taking
Stock from the hands of present holders,
would not o fly prevent collectirm of as
sessment but remove liability for $70 per
share still unpaid on the subscription con
tracts.
Tassajara Without Mail.
PLEASANTON. July 11. -A general tie
up of the Tassajara mail exists,- owing to
the fact that the contract made by J. A.
Harris a year ago expired on July 1. This
mail is transported by carrier between
I'leasanton and the Tassajara postoffice
dally, the distance being about twelve
miles. The contract was sublet to Harris
last year by the original contractor, re
siding in Sedalia, Ohio, who has the con
tract for a four-year term. Harris refuses
to renew his contract and no one else
cares to take it at the figureâ s24o a year.

ate in 1858, died to-day in his ninety-third
year.

A Tannery Destroyed by Fire.
An alarm was turned in from box 27(5
at 11:55 a. m. yesterday for a fire in a
two-story frame building nn Oakwnnd
street, near Eighteenth, owned by Mrs.
< 'ooke and occupied as a tannery by Mr.
Kelly. The fire consumed the entire
structure despite the energetic work of
the department. A second alarm from the
sami' boa was turned in while the fire
was in progress, sparks having ijrmt^d
the roof of a building at T'm Valencia
street. The damage to the latter building
was nominal.
