THE DOLLAR LIMIT.
THE CALL has repeatedly invited the public at
tention to the fact that, in the municipal cam
paign of 1898, each political organization and
every candidate for a municipal office was pledged
to a rate of taxation for this fiscal year that should
not exceed one dollar in the hundred on an assumed
valuation of three hundred and fifty millions. This
pledge, simple, intelligible, incapable of evasion, was
emphatically the special capital of the fusion candi
date for Mayor and of the organ that supported him.
Every political idea that was incorporated into the
fusion platform was stamped into dust by the heels
of the voters. The one thing that lifted Mayor Phe
lan out of the crash and the ruin that befell his party
was his economical pledge, backed, as he expressed it,
by his official experience for two years.
There can be nothing simpler than a man's word.
He either keeps it or he breaks it. This was evident
ly the view of Mayor Phelan when he delivered his
second inaugural before the present Board of Super
visors. Now what is the meaning of the concerted
effort, pressed from a dozen quarters, to force a vio
lation of this pledge? The Republican members of
the board, fully sustained by the Republican press,
have refused even to consider any proposal to recede
from the obligation, either in the letter or in the
spirit. But the Democratic members show an in
clination to fall down. And Mayor Phelan, the Jupi
ter Tonans of the dollar limit, is oft on his vacation,
and. however much he may be entitled to rest, at a
' most critical moment palpably evades a situa
tion which, beyond all other public officials, he has
assisted to create. He has been invited, over and
over again, to reconcile his promises of public im
provements with his economical declarations, and to
make practical suggestions so as to avoid the emas
culation of any department of the municipal service.
But at the precise instant when he is wanted, just
when he could prove the reality of his position by re
ducing the generalities of a canvass to a business
level, he is absent.
The situation, especially on the fusion or Demo
cratic side, is unique. The Democratic Assessor be
lieves that under the new charter a rate of eighty
five cents on the one hundred dollars or thereabouts
ought to produce all the money that the municipality
needs to raise by taxation. The Republican Auditor,
Mr. Wells, thought that, in view of the frauds con
stantly perpetrated in the purchase of supplies, it
might be impossible to observe the dollar limit and
meet the exigencies of the various departments for
the present fiscal year. The real question seems to be
one of equalization, not only as respects the tax
payers, but in the division of the taxes. No citizen
is desirous to see the Fire Department, which has
done such excellent work in the past and upon which
the community so largely depends, reduced to a pov
erty that would impair its efficiency. But, on the
other hand, no citizen who has studied the facts con
siders that this is necessary. Let business methods
be employed, let the purchase of supplies be regulated
by ordinary rules and not used as a mode of sur
rendering public money without a just equivalent,
and, as Mr. Wells has logically proved, the appro
priations of past years can be largely reduced.
The fact is, and the community is impressed with
the belief, that this municipality can be easily run
within the dollar limit upon the valuation of three
hundred and fifty millions. There is no actual need
for all the fuss and disturbance that a simple proposal
to be commonly honest has engendered. The Super
visors themselves, with the aid of other municipal
officers, can scale down and apportion the appropria
tions so that the last half of this year and the first
half of next year will signalize a new departure that
involves' neither more nor less than decent treatment
of the people by the officers they have elected.
But the fusion organ and the fusion Supervisors
ought to stand up to the rack. And, in some way,
even at the expense of a slight curtailment of his va
cation, the benefit of Mayor Phelan's experience
should be obtained and he should be afforded a full
opportunity to fulfill his ante-election promises and to
prove that he has not done his political talking
through his hat.
Leon Dennery, true to the instincts developed un
der the wing of Dan Burns, has carried the habits of
the hoodlum into the offices of the Board of Health.
He tried a few of them yesterday on a caller. Now he
is having his face mended,
THE TRUTH ABOUT CUBA.
WE have recently referred to the attitude of the
United States, toward Cuba and to the opera
tions of the Cuban-American League, with
headquarters in New York. Much light is thrown on
this subject by an article in the current number of the
North American Review. The writer withholds his
name and signs himself, "One of the Army of Occu
! pation in Cuba."
He is evidently an army officer of high intelligence
| and one who clearly comprehends a soldier's duty to
| involve the upholding of his country's honor, in bat
' tie, in pledge-keeping, and in administration.
He says: "There can be no doubt that the pacifi
| cation of the island is now accomplished. City for
; city the towns of Cuba are more peaceful and or
j derly than those of the United States. There never
; was a more docile, quiet people. All the reports of
; brigandage are zealously forwarded to the United
; States, and half of them are lies. The Cubans can
! and will give themselves as good a government as
! have the petty republics of Central and South
â  America. They are impatient to undertake the con
i trol of their own affairs. The irritation of the Cubans
j against Americans and American military rule is daily
I increasing, and will inevitably presently find vent in
a revolt. To conclude, we are in a false position in
Cuba, a position which in its outcome may result in
| wounding our national pride. We have the choice of
two alternatives: Shall we drift, or shall we decide?
To decide; to carry out our promise at once; to take
our troops out of Cuba, leaving the people to gov
ern themselves until such time as they may prefer
annexation â that seems to be our proper course. To
drift means a struggle with a tremendous problem
J under disheartening conditions, trying to help this
people in the face of daily increasing opposition, in
gratitude, irritation, suspicion. It means the postpone
i ment of the settlement of problems connected with
! the material prosperity of the island. It means a
failure to fulfill a solemn pledge. Judging from what
we know of the ease with which revolutions are got
ten up in Spanish-American countries, to drift may |
eventually mean a revolt. And no matter how large
or how small a revolt against us in Cuba might be (it
often happens that the smallest bands are the most
! difficult to deal with), no glory would be ours in
: quelling it, whether we lost in the struggle tens, or
i thousands, or, like the Spaniards, tens of thousands,
: of men. And it will be time then for the enemies of our
1 republic to laugh, for the' Spanish will seem to have
I made good their claim that the war of 1808 was one
j o; pure aggression, inspired by the lust of territory.
It is certain that the question of Cuba overshadows in
gravity any other question now confronting this
country."
Coming from an officer who feels that the least
glorious of wars is that in which for greed the strong
crush the weak, these words of warning should be
J heard and quickly heeded.
Within a month The Call has presented a similar
i view of the same situation. The Cuban-American
League is using a bottomless fund of money in blunt
ing the American -conscience, and, as this soldier
says, by lies, seeking to make justification for viola
tion of the promises of Congress.
One of the highest evidences of the capacity and
cesirc of a people for self-government is their revolt
against military rule. That ours in Cuba is as dis
tasteful as that of Spain, to an unwilling people, is
proved by every sign visible on the island. This
member of our army of occupation sees it and feels
it, and sees that the most distasteful duty of a re
publican soldier is the holding of a protesting people
%in subjection until their friendship is turned to en
mity.
It is evidently the plan of the American pledge
breakers, who seek to tarnish their country's honor
in order to fill their pockets with spoils, to drift until
a revolt shall give excuse for establishing a pseudo
electorate, which will support annexation, while the
majority of the people are pinned down by American
bayonets and denied a voice in the disposition of their
country, their persons and their property.
It is time that those who have the power only to
carry out the orders of Congress and not to disobey
them should make a plain declaration to our own peo
ple and to the Cubans. We are already openly violat
ing the spirit of the Congressional declaration in
Porto Rico and the Philippines. Are we to add to
this the immeasurable dishonor of violating its letter
and spirit in Cuba?
Eighteen months ago great meetings were being
held in our cities, from Boston to San Francisco, in
favor of free and self-governing Cuba. Money was
contributed to free a people whom we are now hold
ing in the slavery of military rule. Would it not be
well to read again the speeches that were then being
made and the fervid resolutions then being passed,
and reassemble those patriotic gatherings to renew
the pledge we took for the independence and self
government of Cuba?
THE QISASTER AT NEWMAN.
BY the disastrous train wreck at Newman we are
brought once more to consider how far careless
persons are to be held responsible for the con
sequences of their carelessness. No one planned the
collision by which two people lost their lives and
thirteen were injured. It was an accident. There
remains, however, the question whether the accident
was unavoidable, or whether some one is to blame
for it.
Pursuing its usual policy, the railroad will of course
exert all its powers to prevent the public from learn
ing the truth of the circumstances under which the
accident occurred. The press will of course gather
evidence as best it can. It has already gathered
much, and doubtless will make known. all the essen
tial facts of the- calamity, but that will not be enough.
There should be a legal investigation, by which there
should be brought' out under oath testimony showing
exactly how the disaster occurred and who is respon
sible for it.
Two features of the accident show negligence car
ried to the extent of recklessness. First, the special
train was made up with the tourist sleeper next to
the tender of tliÂ£ locomotive, not a single baggage
or other car being interposed between them to break
the shock upon the sleeper in case of just such a col
lision as that which occurred. Second, the freight
train stood unflagge<J on the main track, with the
locomotive at the water tank, and the station signal
showed white, which means "clear track." Somebody
is responsible for the reckless way in which the train
was made up, and somebody is to blame for the dis
play of a clear track signal when the track was not
clear.
When questioned by a representative of The Call
as to the condition of the signal lights, the station
master said: "The station signals were out correct,
and if there is any blame it lies with those in charge
of the trains. My report will be made to my su
periors and to no one else.'.' He declined to even
state whether or not the red light should have been
displayed with a freight train on the main track and
a through special due at any moment.
It is not to be doubted that other officers and em
ployes of the road will do as the station-master has
THE SAN FRANCISCO CALL,, WEDNESDAY, JULY 12, 1899.
done. They have stan-ding orders to that effect. Re
ports will be sent to the railroad managers. The in
vestigation by those officials will be a secret one; the
public will be kept in ignorance as far as the railroad
can do it. It is not unlikely they will try to get the
disaster disposed of as an "unavoidable accident," for
! which no one is to blame, and for which the railway
management cannot be held responsible.
It is against public policy to permit serious dis
asters to be set aside in such a summary way. No
one can have any desire to condemn any of the sub
ordinate employes of the road, who have the misfor
tune to be involved in the calamity, but who are in
r.o way responsible for it. For the purpose, there
fore, of ridding innocent persons of unjust suspicion,
the law should use its powers in disclosing the truth
and compelling the railroad officials to reveal what
they know of it. A comprehensive and penetrating
examination ought to be a part of the Corojier's in
quest. The cause of a calamity so dreadful should
not remain the secret of a railroad bureau.
THE REPUBLICAN PRIMARIES.
BY the force and the universality of the protests
made against the policy of voting a blanket
ticket at the primary elections the members of
the Republican County Committee have been duly
informed of the extent of their blunder in devising
such a policy. It is not too late for them to correct
the error. When the petition for the blanket ticket
comes up for consideration by the Election Commis
sioners the committee can withdraw the petition and
leave it for the Commissioners to arrange for the
elections in accordance with law.
â¢ If such a course be adopted the committee will go
far toward setting itself right before the public, and
it is but reasonable to presume it will do so. Noth
ing can be gained in politics by defying public
opinion. There is no profit to a party in alienating
the confidence of the rank and file on the very eve
of a campaign. At the time the blanket ticket scheme
â¢was devised the members of the committee -may not
have been aware of the opposition to it within the
party ranks. They are now, however, fully aware of
it, and not to withdraw it would be to persist in a
blunder even when known to be such.
In addition to the popular antagonism to the
scheme there is the further consideration that in the
opinion of many able lawyers a blanket ticket would
be invalid. That issue will be raised before the Elec
tion Commissioners if an attempt be made to uphold
the petition. There is no need for raising it. The
whole matter can be cleared by a withdrawal of the
ill-devised petition and a request that the Commis
sioners provide for the election by Assembly districts.
When once the blanket ticket scheme has been dis
posed of, the interest of the public will center in the
choice of Assembly district candidates for the county
convention. The very fact that the election of dele
gates is to be made by districts will tend to bring
about the election of truly representative men. It
â will not be so easy to procure the choice of unfit men
ct machine politicians by district voting as it would
have been under the blanket system. That is one of
the chief reasons why the better element of the party
has demanded election by districts, and it now re
mains for that element to profit by the advantage.
Every municipal election involves some issue of
notable importance to the community, but this one
has many such issues. Upon the officers who are to
be elected will devolve the duly of organizing the
government under the new charter and inaugurating
the new system of administration. It ' cannot be
doubted that it will be to the best interest of San
Francisco if that duty be intrusted to the Republican
party, nor is it to be disputed that the independent
"voters will gladly support the Republican candidates
if they are truly representative of genuine Repub
licanism. They will not support the ticket if it be
made up of men who represent only the domination
of the railroad and the cunning of Burns.
A clean ticket means a clean victory, but any other
kind means foul defeat. Let us have delegates to the
convention chosen by Assembly districts, worthy rep
resentatives of Republicanism, and a strong and
trustworthy ticket. Then the contest at the polls
need not be feared. In this issue the Republican
party can afford to defy the railroad and the bosses,
but it cannot afford to offend the people.
CALIFORNIA hardly needs make any elaborate
preparations to welcome the Oregon volunteers
when they arrive on their return from the Phil
ippines. â¢ The spontaneous enthusiasm of the people
for the heroes of the war with Spain, their pride in
the manhood of the Pacific Coast, and their general
delight in hospitality, are in themselves sufficient to
assure to the returning Oregonians, if the Govern
ment permit them to land here, a welcome that will
be notable in the annals of the city â one that the vol
unteers will talk of in their northern homes for years
to come.
Hardly less intense, and none the less cordial, than
the welcome which awaits the volunteers is that which
is extended to the Governor of Oregon and his mili
tary staff, who. have come to this city to receive the
heroes on their arrival. As Governor Geer has made
his visit informally, there will be of course, in ac
cordance with his wish, no public demonstration in
his honor. From the National Guard of the State
and from the State and civic officials, as \<fcll as from
private citizens, however, he will receive abundant
courtesies and hospitalities to attest the honor with
which California regards Oregon, and to prove the
thoroughness with which we sympathize with the
mission that brings him here.
Pacific Coast men, no matter from what State they
h,ail, can never feel other than at home in this city.
As the metropolis of this large section of the world,
San Francisco draws to herself enterprising men from
all the surrounding States, and they bring with them
the patriotisms of the localities from which they come.
Thus there is a strong . Oregon sentiment in San
Francisco, and Governor Geer and his gallant staff
can feel the influence of it in the welcome accorded
them. The record made by the Oregon -volunteers
during the war has added much to the prestige of the
Pacific Coast, and California, as the empire State of
the coast, can have nothing but welcome and hos
pitality for the volunteers themselves and for their
honored Governor.

at any cost of the Secretary of War, is headed, "He
Will Take the Bull by the Horns." Why will some

scandal?

made to pay the penalty of idiocy, if not that of crime.
With the fear of that punishment he might point the
gun at himself first.

like charity, designed to cover a multitude of sins.
All the fashion magazines agree that divorce suits

GENUINE SEA SERPENT
IN NEW YORK AQUARIUM
As the Sea Serpent Looks in the Tanks of the Aquarium.
Sketched From Life.
THE real thins in a BÂ«a serpent was added yesterday to the collection of fish
and reptiles at the aquarium in Battery Park, says the New York World.
It is known as a preen moray. and is the first of its kind ever exhibited in
this part of the country. In the West Indies, where it makes its home
among the coral reefs at the bottom of the ocean, it is known to the natives as
the devil's terror. They stand in great awe of these creatures, which are as
ugly as they are powerful and which are believed by many to be superhuman.
Curator 1,. B, ST>encer of the aquarium received the sea serpent yesterday
with nearly 100 other varieties of fish sent from the West Indies on the steamer
Orinoco. It was placed in one of the exhibition tanks in the afternoon and was
surrounded by a crowd Until closing time. The serpent is ahout seven feet lons
and about twenty-four inches in circumference. It has a sharp snout and nar
row jaws with small eyes set well down toward the point of the snout. Its
teeth are iike needles and slant backward, so that once it has its prey in its mouth
there is no escape.
The great strength of the beast and its, quickness and speed in the water lie in
a bmad dorsal tin extending the entire length of. its back. It belongs to the eel
family and lives mostly on smaller fish and crustaceans. The head in . certain
positions suggested the typical witch's head.
"We had great difficulty," said Curator Spencer, "in securing a green moray
because the natives of the West Indies stand in such terror of them. As it was,
we only got this one by offering a bonus for its capture. I am not sure how it
was caught, but I presume it was in a trap built on the same principle as a lob
ster pot. It showed the wonderful strength of its jaws while being transferred
to the steamer by snapping off the end of a plank that was placed over the tank
to prevent Its escape. The natives claim the bite of the fish is deadly poison,
but I am not so sure of that. We shall know more about the creature after we
have hnd him a little -longer."
Of the other specimens received many were rare. Their colors were as bril
liant and varied as those Â«f the birds of the tropics. Some were extremely oddâ
the' Scotch porgle, with his plaid skin; the cowflsh, with square, horned head,
and the "surgeon," who carries a sharp knife of horn in a groove in his side,
which he shoots out viciously when angered.
"It is the finest shipment of specimens we have ever received," said Professor
Spencer.

NEW PRESIDENT
OF STATE UNIVERSITY
BERKELEY, Cal., July 10. I*o9.
Editor The Callâ Dear Sir: As r.n.
alumnus of the University of California
and a reader of The *Call I wish to thank
you for the right stand you have taken
on the question of securing an able pres
ident for the university.
I hope that you will continue to assist
the members of the Board of Regents,
who are endeavoring to invest the presi
dency with adequate powers, and that
you will lose no opportunity to impress
on the recalcitrant members that by their
actions they are forcing the people of the
State, whether justly or unjustly, to re
gard them as reserving the power of ap
pointing for the benefit of their friends
and followers.
It is but reasonable to suppose that a
president whose whole time is spent in
overseeing the work of the faculty will he
better acquainted with the qualifications
of individuals on that staff than will be
a Board of Regents whose time and at
tention are absorbed in matters of their
own business.
Moreover, as his whole reputation de
pends on the effectiveness of the corps
which he directs, he may safely be trusted
with the best 4nterests of the university.
Yours respectfully. " CALIFORNIAN."

BURGLARS AT OCEAN BEACH.
They Use a Wagon to Carry Away
the Articles Stolen.
Two enterprising burglars went ont
Monday night to the Ocean Beach with a
wagon and visited a number of the houses
In the locality. Mrs. Freda Krueger
heard them trying to force open a window
and she notified her father. He got out
of bed and after dressing himself went
out in search of the burglars.
They did not wait for his coming, but

LAKEPORT CONSECRATED
TAKKI iRT, July 11.â Sunday was a red-letter day in the history of the church in Lake County. The occasion
official visit of the Right Rev. William H. Moreland, Bishop of Sacramento, for the purpose of
â¢ ntly completed building of St. John's Protestant Episcopal Church.
1 j ding is the design of Willis Polk, the architect, of San Francisco, and is completed in the latest
ed style of ecclesiastical architecture. The building is Gothic, the exterior and interior appoint-
Bt bt-autiful character.
The ceremonies consisted of an early communion service at 8 o'clock in the forenoon. The regular consecration
Â til o'clock, a large audience thronging the church. Bishop Moreland conducted the service,
ted bj Rev, B. D. Sinclair, the rector in charge. Morning prayer was first said, the vested choir, Mrs. W. B.
st, and H. V. Keeling, choirmaster, entering in procession. The choir consisted of the following mem-
Mrs. H. V. Keeling. Miss Celia Atherton, Miss Myrtle Hobbs, sopranos; Mrs. Maud Swayza, Miss Sara Col-
El. Chamberlin, altos; Messrs. H. V. Keeling and N. John Lake, tenors; J. Banks, Alvord Schrqeder,
Fred Loring and George Prier, bassos.
riate lessons were read, and Thomas' "Te Deum" and Handel's "Jubilate in G" -were rendered by the
choir with fine effect.
H. V. X Ld the instrument of donation, and Rev. B. D. Sinclair the sentence of consecration, signed and
sealed by Bishop Moreland. The communion office followed, the Bishop being the celebrant, assisted by the rector.
Bishop More-land preached a masterly discourse on "Reverence My Sancuary."
At the evening service, at which another large audience was present, the Bishop again preached, and a class of
seven were confirmed. In response to the appeal of the rector a generous friend donated a $110 bell, and other
friend? a memorial baptism font.
The new church starts off with the most auspicious prospects, and rector and people are full of enthusiasm.
Bishop Moreland made a fine impression, and" large offerings were made. The officers and organization of the
church are to be perfected to-day.

plug was used. Heretofore with guns

ENACTING THE GOLD
STANDARD INTO LAW
; It Is Believed That Democrats in Con
gress Will Champion the Sil
ver Issue in Vain.
! WASHINGTON, July 11.â Another Con
i gressional contest over the silver issue
i will be the result of the determination
I of the Republican caucus committee to
) report in favor of enacting the gold stand
' ard Into law. Among the few Democratic
; politicians now at Washington it is de
, dared that the issue will be cheerfully
| mot. Followers of Mr. Bryan will be
cularly pleased to have the issue
brought to* the front again, and there is
every pr^pp''""! of a heated debate in both
bouses of Congress.
Though Senator Chandler has declared
i his unwillingness to support any measure
i that makes the gold standard a law and
! takf-s it out of the power of the Demo
i cratlc or Populist President to bring the
; country to a silver basis, it is believed he
I will have few followers among the
[ straight-out Republicans. Some Senators
[ may insist on a declaration in favor of
the remonetization of silver by interna
tional agreement being incorporated in
any measure passed, and it Is highly
probable that such declaration may be
put into the proposed bill, but it will have
no effect in law and will be nothing more
than an expression of opinion.
The chances for an international agree
ment which were slim at the beginning
of the present administration, have grown
much less. The enormous increase in the
gold production of the world and evi
dence* that the increase Is to continue
have removed the strongest argument
that free silver men had that there was
not enough gold in the world to form the
basis of money systems.
Another Revere blow to the advocates
of' an international agreement is the an
nouncement from London that the British
Parliamentary committee on Indian cur
rency has reported in favor of the estab
lishment of a gold standard In India.
HARDSHIP ON SHEEPMEN.
Stanislaus Reservation Law Is Bit
terly Denounced.
SAN ANDREAS, July 11.â Assemblyman
Harvey S. Blood of this county, who waa
In San Andreas to-day, spoke unsparingly
in his denunciation of the Stanislaus res
ervation law. This law is provoking se
vere criticism by sheepmen and cattle
men. Alpine, Amador, Calaveras and
Tuolumne counties are the ones made to
suffer by this discrimination. Company A
of United States Cavalry is upon the res
ervation and mounted guards are contin
ually traversing the line, ordering back
the sheepmen. Cattlemen are allowed to
pass this year, but are warned against
coming next year. As the valleys will not
sustain the surplus herds of sheep and
cattle the law will cause hardship. Stock
men say they will go out of business, as
they will not be allowed to feed their
stock upon their own grounds in the
mountains. The Government has offered
to exchange land with those who are de
prived of thia right for other Government
laud outside the reservation. I

COUNTRY EDITORS
SCORE A VICTORY
I Win a Santa Clara Test
Case.
PUBLICATION OF TAX LISTS
SUPERVISORS MAY PATRONIZE
EACH PAPER.
Judge Lorig-an Decides They Have
the Authority to Place the
Printing With Whom
They Choose.
Special Dispatch to The Call.
I SAX JOSE, July 11.â The delinquent tax
I list will be "farmed out" among the varl
; ous weekly papers of the county by the
I Board of Supervisors. This was declared
to be a legal charge against the county by
Judge JLorigan to-day, and the proprietors
of some fifteen country weeklies will be
gainers of sums ranging from $16 to over
j $50. The decision was given in a suit
t brought in the Superior Court by S. T.
! Moore to compel County Auditor Morrison,
to pay a claim of $16 assigned to the
plaintiff by George A. Edes, proprietor of
I the Morgan Hill Sun.
For several years it has been the custom
! of the Board of Supervisors to order the
i publication of portions of the tax list of
I various townships in papers located in the
i townships, in addition to the publication
lin the county official paper. Some of tho
! taxpayers have objected to this, and this
i year they called District Attorney Camp
i bell's attention to the matter. This oili
i cial notified the Supervisors that it could
j not be made a legal charge against the
county, and warned the Auditor against
paying the claims. Moore then sued out a
writ of mandate compelling the Auditor to
pay the claims of the Morgan Hill Sun.
in his decision Judge Lorigan says:
"I nder the statute the Tax Collector ia
charged with the dut^ of publishing such
1 entire list as the official and legal publica
i tion but his duty to so select and pub
i lish is not exclusive. It does not preclude
the Board of Supervisors from directing
such further publication as will in the
i judgment of that body further the pur
i pose of giving notice to the taxpayers of
! their delinquency. A little reflection will
â¢ make it apparent that the printing of por-
I tions of the delinquent list in papers pub
lished in the various townships of tho
county is in harmony with a full discharge
of the duties of the Board of Supervisors,
and for the benefit of the county.
"The country has a particular interest in
seeing that its revenues are collected by
the taxpayers paying their portion of the
I assessed taxes, and as notice of delin
quency most, likely to be brought to the
attention of the taxpayers is designed to
accomplish that result, reasonable efforts
made in that direction by the Supervisors
are proper. It is obvious that this object
is best accomplished by the publication in
those papers which are published in tho
immediate vicinity of the residence of the
taxpayers, and the location of the prop
erty upon which the taxes are delinquent.
I am of the opinion that the Board of Su
pervisors had power to order the publica
tion."
Great interest centered in the case. The
Board of Supervisors had placed the price
of publication of the tax list at 86 cents
per name.
Nickel Machines Must Go.
SAN RAFAEL. July 11.â The nickcl-in
the-slot machine is doomed in this city.
A few weeks ago an ordinance was drawn
up by City Attorney Thomas P. Boyd and
Attorney Henry Eickhoff and presented to
the Board of Town Trustees for its con
sideration, making it a misdemeanor for
any person to have a slot machine about
his place for public use. It is said sev
eral business men who made use of the
machine were in favor of the adoption of
the ordinance on account of the demoral
izing effects of the machine on the youths
cf the community. Last night the ordi
nance was unanimously adopted by the
board, to go into effect next Thursday.

DENNERY MIXES
MATTERS WITH
ALFRED PATTON
Disgraceful Brawl at
Health Office.
NO BLOOD WAS SPILLED
QUARREL OCCURRED OVER A
SALARY DEMAND.
The Enraged Secretary Gave the Ex
rood Inspector Three Vicious
Slaps on the Wrist and
Friends Broke In.
In the picturesque but inelegant lan
guage of Port Warden Kenneally, hi.-;
friend Leon Dennery, secretary to the j
Board of Health, has "slopped over." The j
hot Dennery blood got the upper hand y> B
terday, and the hero of a dozen Tender
loin brawls sailed into ex-Food Inspector
A. T. Patton, struck him in the face with
a wad of paper and slapped him fiercely
on both wrists. The affair might have
been worse, but friends intervened.
Patton went to law over his discharge i
by the last Board of Health and got a j
verdict from the court ordering the board I
to reinstate him. The board, however, j
appealed, but Putton, as a matter of best !
policy, continued to report for duty in
the morning and leave his salary demand
at the end of the month. i
He turned up yesterday morning and of
fered his usual demand to Dennery. Den- j
nery declined to receive it. Then Patton
went to Garret McEnerney, who said he
would write a note to the secretary In i JC
planation. In the afternoon Patton again
went to Dennery and, tendered his demand. ;
It was again refused, and he tossed it on |
Dennery"s desk and turned to leave tho j
room. i
The fire-eating secretary came to the â 
conclusion that it was time to make
known that the dignity of the push could
i not be trifled with, and grabbing the de
i mand he leaped at Patton and with the ;
i folded paper slapped him a heavy blow on !
the left ear. Patton turned to see wl at
was going on, and saw Dennery arranging
his necktie for another swat. He antici
pated it with a stiff punch on the Jaw.
Though Dennery's breath was coming in
short pants from his first exertion he waa
by no means out, and succeeded in return-
Ing a hard right and left on each of Pat
ton's wrists. The battle was carried Into
the hall, where a crowd of jeering onlook
ers quickly gathered. No one interfered
until Dennery attempted to foul Patton
with a short-arm jab on the ankle, when
friends stepped in and put a stop to fur
ther damage.
The spectacle was hardly edifying from i
â  any point of view. The angry secretary |
] made use of about all the billingsgate he [
could muster after his late campaign with
Colonel Burns, and Patton was not trail- j
ing far behind him. Even after the two !
men were separated they continued to j
curse each other until dragged away In
different directions. That the row did not <
take on a more serious aspect is remark
! able, as Dennery is known as a vicious
! lighter under certain circumstances.
There were no arrests.
A NOTABLE EVENT
Monster Family Excursion to Santa
Cruz.
SPLENDID TRAINS OF FIRST
CLASS COACHES AND A
VERY LOW RATE
OF FARE.
A Good Opportunity to Enjoy the
Many Attractions of the City
by the Sea.
The Southern Pacific Company an
nounces that Sunday, the 23d inst., is
the date set apart for the great annual
excursion to Santa Cruz, and which
will enable excursionists to visit this
beautiful city by the sea during the
height of the summer season and par
ticipate with the thousands of other
visitors in the enjoyment of swimming,
boating, driving, etc.
Another very important attraction at
Santa Cruz at this time will be the
beautiful camp of the League of the
! Cross Cadets, numbering over 500.
As heretofore this great excursion
will be under the personal supervision
of Colonel William H. Mtnton, the
I well-known excursion passenger agent
of the Southern Pacific Company, who
will see to it that the excursionists are
well cared for.
These trains (the excursion being
run probably in three sections) will be
| run over the scenic narrow-gauge route
through the famous Santa Clara Val
ley and along the beautiful streams
which wind through the magnificent
redwood forests of the Santa Cruz
Mountains.
The round-trip rate for this occasion
has been placed at the very low price
r.f $2. and special excursion tickets will
be placed on sale at the Grand Hotel
ticket office on July 19, 20. 21 and 22.
and at the depot ticket office on the
morning of the excursion. Those in
tending to avail themselves of this op
portunity will take the 7:45 a. m. boat
from the San Francisco ferry landing.
Train leaves Fourteenth and Franklin
streets at 7:45 a. m. and from Alameda
(Park street) at 8:20 a. m. Returning,
excursionists will be landed at San
Francisco at 8:05 p. m.
SECURED A NEW TRIAL.
Supreme Court Awards Tax Collector
Johnson of Mendocino County
Another Hearing.
Ex-Tax Collector J. R. Johnson of Men
clno County was granted a new trial yes
terday by the Supreme Court. As Tax
Collector of the northern county it is al
leged he collected three thousand dollars
which was not turned into the county
treasury. Suit was brought by the coun
ty to recover the amount from his bonds
men and a decision was rendered against
the official. He appealed and the Supreme
Court reversed the judgment because the
court excluded evidence of certain sums
of money that he paid into the treasury,
which were shown by the Treasurer's re
ceipts. The reason given by the lower
court for excluding the testimony was
that it would not do the defendant any
good, because the plaintiff could by
amending his complaint charge and credit
the plaintiff with all sums collected by
him and paid into the treasury and there
would still be the three thousand dollar
item unaccounted for.
The Supreme Court held that the rejec
tion of evidence that was admittedly com
petent could not be satisfied by any such
ground. It, therefore, granted the ap
pellant a new trial.

i tion of the Epworth League of the Mctho
| dist Episcopal Church, which convenes In
i Indianapolis next Wednesday, were given
an enthusiastic- farewell at the Central
Methodist Episcopal Church on Mission
street mar Seventh last evening. The
California delegation as a whole will work
. to secure the convention of 1901 for Call
! fornia, the question of whether it shall be
j held in San Francisco or Los Angeles to
be left for future decision as a matter of
I secondary importance.
The vestry of the church, in which the
j meeting was held, was well filled, among
those present being a number of Leaguers
from the towns on the other side of the

the interior, all anxious to testify their
interest in the movement and furnish in
spiration to the delegates on the eve of
', their departure on their important mis
sion, both by their presence and their
1 words of cheer.
C. H. Perkins presided and opened tne
! proceedings with a felicitous address
; which ;>ut everybody in good humor and
prepared the way for those who followed
him. Nearly all the delegates, with the
j exception of the Rev. Dr. W. W. Case,
j who is in the East, were present and oc
: cupied st-ats near the platform.

| first speaker and he declared that Cali
fornia, as one of the strongholds of the
leaguers, was a good place to hold the an
nual convention, and he had no doubt that
: the effort to bring it here would be suc
cessful if the proper amount of energy
' were expended in that direction. He was
j followed by Vi. H. Waste of Oakland,
!Dr Bovard, Mr. Morton of San Jose,
Mr Alexander of Livermore. T. B. Hutch
inson and Dr. "Williams of Napa. Dr. Ftl
i ben. Dr. J. N. Beard and Dr. Locke, All
the' speeches were short and all were
brim-full of eloquence and aroused un
bounded enthusiasm. Dr. Beard urged
everybody who was interested in the Cal
| iforriia branch of the league to give the
i delegates every assistance in his power,
I not only in words of encouragement be
fore their departure, but in letters of en
i eouragement after their arrival on the
' rield at Indianapolis.
Mr. Perkins announced that although
I Dr. Case was not present he had sent
word that the Leaguers of the Northwest,
or that part of the country formerly
known as the Northwest, up around St.
Paul and Minneapolis, were going to the
convention shouting "California and 1901."
and the announcement was received with
a burst of applause.
The delegates from the northern section
of the State are: Dr. W. W. Case, Dr. J.
N. Beard, Dr. Thomas Filben, Dr. J. C.
Simmons Dr. Thomas Boyers, Henry
i Morton, T. B. Hutchinson, Louis Norton
i and W. H. "Waste. Four of the delegates
! will leave for the East to-night and the
i remaining delegates will get away the last
j of the week.

HAS NOT LOST

White.

From the Sixth Round to the Finish
He Is Kept Constantly On
the Defensive by the
Champion.
Special Dispatch to The Call.
DENVER, July 11.â George Dlxon.
featherweight champion of the world.
was given the decision over Tommy
White at the end of a twenty-round
bout before the Olympic Club to-night.
Dlxon led the fighting from the sixth
round to the finish, Tommy's purpose be-
Ing to avoid a knockout and stay the
limit. George's left hand work was the
comment of all. He would bring it from
the ribs to the head and back again so
quickly as to almost escape the eye.
" Tom O'Rourke sat in Dixon's corner
CHAMPION GEORGE DIXON.
and frequently cautioned him when his
cveranxiety would make him careless.
Kid McCoy officiated In the same capacity
for White. Bat IKasterson was referee.
The attendance numbered 2600. j
Dlx.jn weighed in this afternoon at 1> (
pounds and White at 120 pounds. Ihe
betting was 10 to 8 on LMxun and quite j
a bunch of money changed hands at .
these odds. Â
In the first round Dixon forced mat
ters. Tommy blocked cleverly and land- ;

There was no damage. ,- <Â»â-â.â '
The second round was very fast, George
using his left to good advantage. Tommy

left to George's head and his right to the ,
left eve. As the round closed bipod
trickled from a cut over George s left |
C "Yn the third round White was the ag
greasor at first, but ran into a stiff left

rights and lefts that put him on the de
fensive. George became careless and was
cautioned byO'Rnurke, He settled down
and was showering vicious lefts on lorn
mv's body as the round closed.
In the next round <;^rge display. -d
overanxietv and judged distance badl>.
Tommy was cool and careful. Just at
the end George brought his right across
with force to Tommys kidneys and his
left to the neck, staggering \\ hite. Hon- ;
oth were about even.
In the fifth Tommy displayed clever
ducking and side stepping, arid thereby
avoided the fierce rushes of Dlxon. The
latter was trying for a knockout. ,
In the following round Dlxon settled
down to business. His bard lefts on
Tommy's face and body visibly weakened
the latter, who recovered only momen
tarily until the end of the fight, from
this on to the eighteenth round It was
pretty much the same. George was
strong and on the aggressive all the time.
Tommy seemed dazed and led but little.
He hung to George a good deal of the
time But Dlxon could not place enough
fore* in his blows, although he rained
them on Tommy's body and head to put
In the last minute of the twentieth
round George brought a hard eft to
Tommy's neck, which caused him to
waver It looked like the finish, but

DOVE STEW FOR MARKSMEN.
Yuba and Sutter Gun Club Preparing
for Its Outing.
MARYSVTT.L.E. July 11.â The directors
of the Yuba :md Sutter Gun Club are

Special TMspateh to The Call.
SOUTHAMPTON, July II. â ThÂ« Britan
nia having left tho drydock. the Sham
rock has taken her place. All that can
I,? <spon of the cup challenger is her tow
ering topmast, which stands higher than
tho factory chimneys adjoining:. Sh.i is
jealously screened from all observation by
canvas coverings brought over irom Kat
sey's at Cowes.
The sailing spin with the Britannia will
take place one week from to-dny. Th-2
Prince of Wales' yacht has taken the up-
per moorings at Cowes, The people are \
becoming excited about the Shamrock.
All sorts of reports were about to-day,
nearly all of which were false. The crow
had been heard talking, and it was stated
that the yacht was going out again yes
terday, but nothing of the sort occurred.
She had two anchors down all day just
below Meteor, and within easy hailing
distance of her auxiliary, which is used
for the hands to sleep on and as a gen
eral storeroom.
Fays launch was running from the
shore to the Shamrock and back again
most of yesterday, bringing all kinds of
linings which have been found necessary
and the crew was kept hard at work stif
fening up the stays, shrouds and so forth,
and in a general way acquiring the high
ly necessary qualification of becoming at
home and making an Intimate acquain
tance with the ship.
I met a man yesterday who has seen
the construction of her steel mast. He
said it was made In plates fourteen feet
long, each plate- being in half circles and
then welded like the plates of a boiler.
They were polished and then painted. He
says such a mast Is unbreakable, and the
same applies to the gaff and boom. The
mainsail. It Is said, weighs a ton.
Sir Thomas Upton, aboard the Erin,
has taken his guests, Ix>rd and Lady
Clanwilliaxn, Lord and Lady Lovelace and
others away on a cruise. The Britannia
' was away yesterday afternoon, having
1 been recoppered and the gaff and boom
of hollow steel shipped.
The press continues to maintain the
fallacy that the trials between Britan
nia and Shamrock will be very signifl
cant. They will be to those who have an
Inside understanding in the matter, but
not to the public generally. Fife wishes â
and is clever enough â to baffle any pub
lic curiosity.
LONDON, July 11.â Sir Thomas Lipton
this evening informed the correspondent
of the Associated Press that there wa3
absolutely no foundation for the report
that the Shamrock was leaking. He says
she has been docked merely to have her
hull repolishpd, preparatory to the trial
with the Britannia.

SIX SAGEBRUSH
WHEELMEN MAKE
A RAPID JOURNEY
I Record Lowered Over
the Sierras.
CARSON TO SACRAMENTO
, ARE MEMBERS OF THE CARSON
ATHLETIC CLUB.
;R. J. Kelly, George Tranter, Tom
O'Hallorhan, Henry Burlington,
Al Karge and Jacob Mul
ler Tell Experiences.
Six wheelmen of the Carson City Ath
letic Club arrived in this city yesterday
morning yellow with mountain dust, after
having lowered all previous records of
bicycle trips from Carson, over the Sierras
!to Sacramento. The party consisted of
Reuben J. Kelly, George Tranter, Thomas
O'Hallorhan, Henry Burlington, Al Karge
i and Jacob Muller. All are expert wheel
< men and Kelly holds many championship
medals for racing events which have
taken place in the Sagebrush State.
Leaving Carson City. Nev., on Sunday
night about 10 o'clock the boys dismount
; ed in the capital city of California Mon
; day night shortly after 9 o'clock. The
exact time en route was twenty-three
hours and forty-two minutes. They cov
ered a distance of over 200 miles, follow
ing the railroad from Reno over the
mountains. The same journey has been
made before by way of Placerville, which
is a much easier route, by H. B. van Etten
of Carson City in exactly twenty-four
hours. The cyclers, who are in San b ran
cisco, will go on record as having eighteen
minutes advantage over all previous ef
forts between the two points.
In speaking of the trip the iNevadans
say that the poorest road encountered was :
a stretch of thirty-five miles in length be
tween Carson City and Reno. The road
bed forihe remainder of the way was lair
and easy on wheels. The steep climb to
Summit 'up the eastern slope of the moun
tain range consumed most of the time,
the drop clown the western slope over the
foothills being made at a rapid pace with
but few stops. The boys rode almost In
cessantly for the twenty-three hours, ;
without sleep, and hesitating only long i
enough to secure food. The citizens or
their native city turned out to witness
their departure and at Sacramento they
were accorded a royal reception by the
wheelmen of that town.
Each of the tourists wore the natty uni
form of the Carson City Athletic Club,
and when they passed up Market street
early yesterday morning, mounted on
their wheels, their paraphernalia was the
object of much admiration. It consists of |
pale-green Jerseys, on which is the em
blem of the club, with leggins to match, |
a white soft felt hat, bound with a green â 
ribbon. : " â  - .. ,
Before returning to Nevada the boys
will make a tour of the southern part of .
the State on their wheels. They are all
enthusiastic over good roads and are in
terested in a movement which will result
in the improvement of Nevada highways
for cycling purposes. They made a leis- ,
urely trip from Sacramento to Stockton
and thence to this city. The express sat
isfaction at the condition of the roads in
California,

CYCLING DOWN THE SIERRAS.
COURSING OPENING AT INGLESIDE.
LEASHMEN ARE
WAITING FOR THE
MAYOR'S ACTION
Ingleside Gates Ready
to Open.
FRIDAY WILL TELL THE TALE
A WINNERS' STAKE AT UNION
PARK THIS WEEK.
Is the Choice Bit in a Double Pro
gramme â Many Champion
Dogs Will Be Sent to
the Slips.
The members of Ingleside Coursing Club
met last night to discuss the situation re
garding their park. One week ago last
Monday the Supervisors passed an ordi
nance permitting the park to reopen under
the old conditions. Thfs ordinance be
comes a law on Friday night if his Honor
the Mayor signs it or refrains from veto
ing it. Up to the present time he has not
signified his Intention in the matter.
The management of the park has not
been disorganized during the past three
months of inactivity and is reaiiy to re
sume the sport at almost a moment s
notice. If Mayor Phelan does not veto
the ordinance before the close of business
hours on Friday a draw will be held at
Pythian Castle that evening. The stake
will be an open one, unlimited as to en
tries. Already a sufficient number of en
tries have been offered to insure the suc
cess of the meeting.
When the park reopens neither women
nor children will be allowed in ot near
the betting ling. A commodious stand
has been prepared for them and they will
not have access to the enclosure where
the pool boxes are located.
The draw for the two stakes to be run
on Saturday and Sunday at Union Park
will be held to-night in Pythian Castle.
The Winners' Stake will be the tid-bit of
the programme, bringing out the best
dogs in the champion class. The Reserve
Stake Will attract a large entry, the two
making a big oard. On the 23d inst. the
regular monthly Sapling Stake will be
the attraction. With the number of fast
youngsters coming up every day some
surprises are expected.
INSPECTS THE PALO
ALTO STOCK FARM
Japanese Commissioner After Infor
mation Regarding the Breeding
of Horses.
PALO ALTO, July 11.â Two distin
guished representatives of the Jap
anese Government, Count H. Mutsu,
consul at San Francisco, and Inazo
Nit.. be, special commissioner of the De
partment of Agriculture and Commerce
of Tokio, Japan, paid a visit to the Palo
Alto stock farm on the Stanford estate
yesterday. The gentlemen were here to
obtain information' in regard to the
breeding and raising of high-grade
harness horses and to gather facts as
to the profits In the business.
Superintendent Fraoik V. Covey of
the stock farm brought out all the fine
horses, showing the grand results
achieved in breeding according to the
Ideas of the late Senator Stanford. He
explained in detail the points in con
nection with the raising and breeding
of fine stock and said the prices of
high-grade horses had risen quite a
little during the past three years.
Inazo Nitobe, the special commis
sioner, Intimated that a favorable re
port to the Japanese Government
might cause large breeding establish
ments to be begun in his country,
which would benefit Californians ma
terially by supplying a market where
their produce could be disposed of in
large numbers.

WILL UNDERTAKE THE
REFORM OF POLITICS
The Affiliated Republican Clubs Meet
to Effect a Permanent
Organization.
Pursuant to the invitation of the execu
tive committee, a large and enthusiastic
meeting of Republicans, under the avs
pices of the Affiliated Republican Clubs,
was held last evening at the Union League
clubrooms, Palace Hotel. A. O. Perkins
presided and Hamilton J. Riggins acted as
secretary of the league.
The president stated the object of the
meeting to be permanent organization, to
participate in the interests of pure poli
tics, and the selection of representative
Republicans for office at the coming elec
tion. After an exchange of views from
the delegates of the various clubs repre
sented it was resolved that a tempoiary
organization for the coming campaign be
effected.
On mution the president appointed a
committee on temporary organization.
â  when a short recess was taken. The com
1 mittee submitted its report, recommending
that a finance committee of seven and a
committee on constitution and by-lawa
consisting of seven members be appointed,
both committees to organize and report at
the next meeting.
The following gentlemen were appointed
the finance committee: W. G. Taffinder,
F. H. Ryblnson, A. O. Perkins, R. Luna,
j William Offerman, M. F. Taylor, Dr. C.
Ford.
Onimittee on organization and by-laws:
Gaston Straus, Morgan Backus, Dr. D. \V.
McX.Mll. J. B. Desmond, P. D. Hayes, W.
J. Herrin. A. M. Currie.
On motion the secretary was instructed
to notify the affiliated clubs to send dele
to attend the next conference. The
meeting then adjourned, to meet again i.n
Wednesday evening, July 19. at the Union
League clubrooms. Palace Hotel.

The Twenty-Eighth District On the
City's Democracy.
To the wise political district club meet
ings are political barometers. At the reg
ular weekly meeting last evening of the
Buckley Democratic Club No. 1 of the
Twenty-eighth district, the first speaker,
Clltus Barbour, addressed an audience
that filled the hall, Mr. Barbour being the
first speaker. He spok* 5 in the ready and
elontient style characteristic of him. and
. c ;."kr in a diplomatically general way on
the origin and destiny of Democratic
clubs. He said nothing about the factional
quarrels of the Democracy, but the bur
der. of his forcible speet (5 was that the
Democracy Bhould become united again
thrcugr- the agency of the new primary
law and that the defeated one? at the
primary election, whoever they were,
should get in and take their medicine,
whatever it was, as administered by the
I oratic municipal convention, and so
help out party unity. The interest a?
nificance of this speech, which Mr. B,iv
bour has maxle, in effect, before other
Buckley clubs within a few days.
largelj from the fact that he has long
been a fierce opponent of Buckley and
a close supporter of Gavin McNab.

ARRIVES TO BID THE
OREGON BOYS WELCOME
GOVERNOR T. T. GEER.
GOVERNOR OEER, accompanied by his staff, arrived In this city by
rail yesterday morning. The purpose of his visit is to greet the re
turning Oregon volunteers. Immediately on the arrival of the Gov
ernor Adjutant General Beamans of California and Colonel J. F. Bur
gin welcomed the Governor and his staff and extended to them the
hospitality of the State. The distinguished party visited the Bohemian
and Union League clubs, and also paid its respects to General Shatter at the
headquarters of the Department of Caifornia.
In speaking of the proposed welcome to the Oregonians by the people of
San Francisco Governor Geer said he would be _ pleased if the soldiers were
given an opportunity to march through the streets of San Francisco, but ob
served that the question of leaving the ship was one that would have to be de
cided by the military authorities. He remarked that he would consult Colo
nel Somers when the troops arrived, and thought the prospects were good for
the troops leaving the ship for a short time.
Governor Geer said his State was proud of the record It made In the war.
It was gratified by the achievement of the Oregon volunteers in the field, and,
moreover, Oregon was highly delighted by the splendid record made by the
battleship named after the State.
Adjutant General Tuttle, chief of staff of the Governor, has held that
position for several years, having served under Governor Lord aa chief of staff.
Accompanying the Governor are the following members of his staff: Ad
jutant General B. B. Tuttle, Colonel D. M. Dunn, Colonel A. B. Gillis, Colonel
S. C. Spencer, Major J. M. I. Wood and Captain K. C. Mearns.
Last evening Governor Geer and bis staff were entertained by the members
of the staff .if Governor Gage, and to-day they will be_ offered such courtesies
as are compatible with the close watch they are keeping for reports of the
returning transports that are bringing home the brave boys of Oregon whom
they have come such a distance to greet.

In the Forty-Third.
The meeting of Republicans of the
Forty-third Assembly District held last
evening in California Hall, 620 Bush
street, was largely attended. J. R. Hart
was elected temporary chairman and F.
G. Robrecht secretary. Resolutions fa
voring the charter, denouncing the
"blanket ticket" find protesting against

their diÃtrict, or in any part of the city
and county, were adopted. An adjourn
ment was "taken to meet in the same hall
next Wednesday evening.
THE LAMBARDI
COMPANY BIDS
THE CITY ADIEU
WHEN the curtain fell on the
"Barber of Seville" last even
ing at the California Theater
it marked the passing of the
Lambardi Grand Italian Opera
Company, for the presf-nt at least. That
it will soon return there is no doubt; in
fact arrangements for a return engage
ment have practically been settled by
Manager Friedlander of the California.
Saji Francisco awoke to the realization
last night that it was going to lose this
company, which has so favorably im
pressed the lovers of opera of this city,
and the first full house that has greeted
the company since it opened practically
unannounced in the Alhambra and began
Its peregrinations from theater to theater
gathered at the California. Never were
players greeted more warmly than those
of the Lambardi company last night. It
was an ovation, applause from the be
ginning assuming the volume of a din.
The impassioned, graceful singing of Sig
norina Repetto, who assumed the role of
Rosina; the full, round bass of Travaglini.
the spirited tenor of Russo, the clever
acting- of Bergrami and the mellow bary
tone of Vizzardelli threw the audience
into ecstacies. Through the evening the
admirers of the players togged a wealth
of flowers on the stage. Three curtain
calls was the rule of the evening and
the singers, inspired by the appreciation
of their listeners, sang as they have not
since they reached the city. Of
Rossini's opera, "The Barber of Seville,"
much has been said and the people know
the argument weli. but doubtless it will
be many months before it is sung again
in tnis city with such spirit and approval,
unless, Indeed, the same throats tire in
its rendition on their return.
MRS. B. MONTI MIXES
THINGS ON THE STAND
NAUGHTY WIDOWS TESTIMONY
CONTRADICTED.
Lewis Street Residents Tell of the
Unhappy Relations Existing
Between Mrs. Kopp and
Her Husband.
Mrs. Bertha Monti has a bad memory.
What she testified to on the stand in the
Kopp murder case she is apt to forget
less than thirty minutes later, thereby in
volving Judge, attorneys and herself in
much useless argument. Mrs. Monti tes
tified yesterday morning that the only
time she had taken dinner with the mur
dered man was on the evening when Mrs.
Kopp had met them in the restaurant to
gether, the self-same evening that the
wife took his life because she "wanted
him for herself."
Mrs. Catherine Caganelli, proprietress
of the restaurant where Kopp was din
ing with Mrs. Monti on the fatal even
ing, testified that the widow came in fre
quently with Kopp to dinner. In fact,
they came in so frequently that the wit
ness learned to know them.
After Mrs. Caganelli left the stand
Judge Conlan recalled Mrs. Monti to ex
plain the testimony of the witness. "I
dined with Kopp many times," Mrs.
Monti said; "but only once since Mrs.
Kopp'a return from the East."
Mrs. Caganelli was recalled and posi
tively testified that she had seen Kopp
and Mrs. Monti "more than once and
more than twice during the past month
in the restaurant together."
The witnesses examined brought nut
nothing new in regard to the unhappy rela
tions existing between the murdered man
and his wife. Mrs. Minnie Dotterer, Mrs.
Burns Mrs. Julia Vendome. Mrs. Marie
Carlo and Otto Carlo all related incidents
relative to the brutal fashion in which
Kopp was in the habit of addressing his
wife Mrs. Nussbaum told how Mrs.
Kopp and the little girl Amanda had come
to her hungry, saying Kopp refused them
the necessary money for food
Mrs. Sophia Robin, sister of the accused
woman testified that Kopp had acknowl
edged to her that he was intimate with
Mrs. Monti.
All the testimony In the case is now
in and to-day at 11 Mrs. Kopp will take
the stand in her own behalf.

Leg.
Thf police are searching for Ben Sid
lowÂ«ki. a peddler, who is wanted on the
chnrtre of cruelty to animals. It is an

NO NONSENSE IN
DR. WHEELER'S
REQUIREMENTS
Wants No Log Rolling
With Regents.
HE INSPIRES CONFIDENCE:
CHATS WITH OFFICETtS AND
FBIENDS OF UNIVERSITY.
Belief That the Cornell Professor
Will Accept the Presidency of
tie Institution at
Berkeley.
Dr. Benjamin Ide Whaler, president- i
elect of the University of California, ar- â 
rived in the city from New York early ;
yesterday morning and attended a ses
sion of the regents In the afternoon. He j
will be in and about San Francisco for a j
week or ten days and during that time ;
will acquire facts concerning the uni- ,
versity and acsertain what views the ;
regents hold regarding the future man- j
agement of the institution. He may run
down to Los Angeles and pass a day with ;
the school teachers there assembled. Next i
Sunday he will be the guest of Mrs.
Hearst at her country home near Liver
more. She arrived in the city yesterday.
but did not attend the regular session of
the board of regents.
Dufing the forenoon yesterday Dr. j
"Wheeler met several of the regents at
theft respective offices. He chatted for a j
considerable time with General Barnes,
Judge Wallace, Arthur Rodgers, Judge
Waymire and Judge Slack. It is readily
acknowledged by the regents that Pro- i
fessor Wheeler is an impressive and In- I
spiring man. General Barnes fancies that
the students of Berkeley will be delighted j
with the Cornell professor. Before his
present visit ends Dr. Wheeler will in- j
form the regents whether he will accept
or decline the presidency of the uni
versity. In the minds of the regents there I
is hardly a doubt of his acceptance, but j
he desires to talk over affairs personally |
with each member of the board. Of course
these conversations will be In the nature
Of "heart to heart talks" for the good !
of the cause of higher education on this '
Bide of the continent. Dr. Wheeler is in '
no sense destitute of that quality known
as personal magnetism and if he pursues
his plan of campaign, which contemplates j
a personal Interview with each regent, I
he will surely capture the whole board, j
In general conversation yesterday Dr. I
Wheeler remarked: "My requests placed
before the regents are by no means un- |
usual. I do not believe that there should
be any logrolling on the part of any mem
ber of the faculty with the regents in
order to hold his position. I think my
requests have in a measure been misun
derstood. I do not believe in wholesale
decapitations. It is not the question of
one man's place or even two men's places.
Politics should not enter into the question
at all. So long as politics govern ap- |
polntments there will be no peace, no
quiet, no effective advancement."
General Barnes presided at the session
of the board yesterday. The Regents' at
tending were: Hallidie, Denicke, Budd,
Houghton. Wallace. Lieutenant Governor
Xeff, Speaker Alden Anderson. Rodgers,
Slack, Foote, Pardee and Barnes.
The board adopted the following reso
lution:
Resolved, With the concurrence of
the president of the board. Governor
of the State and the Speaker of the
Assembly and the consent of the State
Board of Examiners that the Commit
tee on Audit and Finance is hereby
authorized and directed to advance
from that portion of the university one
cent fund, set aside for permanent im
provements, so much as may be neces
sary to make up any deficit that may
occur in the revenue of the univereity
for current expenses, and to report the
same from time to time to tfct board.
A communication from Professor Wil
liam Cary Jones was received, wherein
was offered a donation of $3000 from Mrs.
Cornelius B. Houghton, to be used as a
scholarship. Mrs. Houghton expressed
the hope that at least $130 per year be
derived therefrom. The donation was ac
cepted and the board tendered its thanks
to the donor.
Director Keeler recommended the ap
pointment of three fellows at the Lick
Observatory, naming Messrs. Coddington,
Palmer, Rays. On motion of Regent Slack
the three gentlemen named were appoint
ed at a salary of $100 per year.
Dr. Pardee moved that General Barnes
be made chairman of the Lick Observa
tory Committee. The motion was carried
unanimously.
The board adjourned to meet next Tues
day, July IS, at 1 p. m., and confer with
Dr. Wheeler.

NOTICE.
Assessment Book of Real and Per-
sonal Property, 1899.
OFFICE OF THE CLERK OF BOARD OF
Supervisors of the City and County of San
Francisco, City Hall building, second floor, July
4, 1599.
Public notice is hereby given, in accordance
with section No. 3654 of the Political Code, that
the Assessment Book of the City and County of
Ban Francisco, for the year 1899, has been com-
pleted and delivered to me, as Cleri of tha
Board of Supervisors, together with the map
books and statements, and will be open lor ex-
amination in this office from 9 o'clock a. m. to
4 o'clock p. m., and that said Board of Super-
visors "met to examine the Assessment Boole
and equalize the assessment of property therein,
on MONDAY AFTERNOON, July 3, 1899, at 2
o'clock and will thereafter continue in session.
from time to time until the business of equal-
ization presented to them is disposed of. but not
later than the 17th day of July, 1839.
Applications for correction of assessment of
real estate and personal property are required
to be verified by oath.
Public notice Is also given, that In accordance
with sections 1897, 1899 and 3654 of the Political
Code, the Military Roll has been completed, de-
livered over to me and is now open for exam-
ination and correction, as provided by law.

FRUIT AND SALMON IN ENGLAND.
"California fruits continue In good demand
at firm rates. For the 1599 pack canners have
â¢withdrawn their offers, but resales have taken !
place at hardening rates," says the London j
Grocers' Gazette, and reports the salmon mar- i
ket aa follows: "There is no quotable change in !
this market. The consumptive demand has im- i
proved Flight!}-, but the Inquiry from first
hands is extremely limited, and as a matter
of fact dealers are in some cases offering at
Jower prices than those demanded by packers'
agents. The position of this market ls at the
present time a somewhat curious one. On the
r tip hand nackers state that prices must go
higher In consequence of the increased i
production, while the demand from the United
States for all classes of tinned provisions ls
at that packers can get their own prices
from that market. Many of the dealers and
wholesale houses here, however, take the op
posite view, being of opinion that the market
ls at present too high, and they support their
opinion by selling' from the stocks they hold
on hand at less than they can purchase at the
present moment."

FORECAST.
The pressure has risen slowly off the coast
cf California. Elsewhere west of the Rocky-
Mountains it remained nearly stationary.
Thunderstorms are reported in Utah and South
ern Idaho.
The temperature has fallen along the central
coast of California. In the great valleys of
California tht- temperatures are about normal.
Thj following maximum wind velocities are
reported: San Francisco, 40 miles per hour,
from the west, and Pocatello, 34 miles, from
the west. Over the northern half of the Pa
cific const warm tair weather prevails.

EASTERN MARKETS
NEW YORK STOCK MARKET.
NEW YORK, July 11.â stock market
â¢trlklngly demonstrated its Inherent strength
to-day with a practical recovery of yester
day's losses. The closing prices to-day were
only slightly below these or Saturday and in
some exceptional cases sharply above. The
only conditions in which any change had taken
place were purely technical. These were the
elimination of a weakly margined long ac
count and the creation of a short Interest.
London began to sell stocks in New York at
the opening to-day as it did yesterday, but
Instead of coming upon a large amount of
nearly exhausted margins, as was the case
yesterday, the opening decline Invited buying
to cover the short contracts put out yesterday.
The buying from this source afforded the prin
cipal support to the market In the early deal-
Ings It - was particularly marked In Sugar
and the New York Traction and Gas stocks,
â which suffered most severely from bear raid-
Ing yesterday. By the time this short Interest
had " been covered the market came to a
standstill, the bulls apparently lacking cour
age to push their advantage in the face of ihe
continued firmness of the money market, which
held quite firmly about the legal rate for call
money. Slightly before noon, however, a de
termined buying movement developed In the
great trunk lines and with occasional Intervals
â preadlnjr from out group to another
until It embraced pretty much the whole
other until It embraced pretty much the whole
list. The agreement among the trunk lines to
restore rates on grain to the seaboard was ap
parently the cause of the origin of the buying
In that group. St. Paul's increase In gross
earnings for the first week In July of $203,822
prompted .the buying of the Grangers. North
ern Pacific scored a brilliant advance, recov
ering more than its dividend, which was taken
off to-day. After a fractional reaction on pro
fessional profit-taking, prices were marked up
again to near the best In the closing dealings.
Bonds rallied somewhat from the early weak
ness in sympathy with stocks, but are slightly
lower on the day. Total sales par value
12,080.000.

CHICAGO GRAIN MARKET. .
CHICAGO, July 11.â September wheat opened
about 4c lower, at 72%@73c. sold between 72% c
and 734 c, closed unchanged at 73c. Fair
weather In the wheat belt and continued large
primary receipts caused a degree of easiness at
the 3tart, and a decline followed the opening.
The decline was soon recovered. There was
irregular buying: by the less confident shorts,
who feared the influence of the Russian crop
failure, fresh confirmatory news of which was
received to-day, and the absence of Belling
pressure of note was sufficient to sustain
prices. The market, however, was exceedingly
narrow and price changes small until after
noon, when trade broadened out somewhat.
Bradstreet reported 178,000 bushels decrease In
the world's visible... and reports were received
of damage to Wisconsin's spring wheat. The
price was advanced to 734 c, the highest point
of the day. Trade became dull again toward
the close, and the pit at times was half
empty, with the easiness usually a feature of
narrow trading.
Corn was dull, but fairly steady. Prices fol
lowed wheat, September closing unchanged.
Oats were extremely dull, but steady. Sep
tember closed Vsc lower than yesterday.
Provisions were firm and fairly active.
Speculative demand, both local and from out
side, was good, and prices averaged slightly
higher all day. Packers were again in the mar
ket. At the close Sectember pork was 10c
Wisher and lard and ribs 5c higher.
The leading futures ranged as follows:

BOSTON WOOL MARKET.
BOSTON, July â The wool market this
week continues strong and values are fully
maintained. The demand Is fairly good, but
represents speculative purchases by dealers as
well as buying for manufacturers. The bulk of
the business doing Is yet in Territory grades.
Including California, Texas and Oregon. The
total sales of these wools for the week footed
up to about 3,500,000 pounds, and the price ob
tained Is better for the good wools. For good
lots containing a fair amount of staple the
market is quotable on the scoured at 48@50c
for fine medium and fine, with choice staple
lines at &2Â©54 c. , ._.._,
Fleeces are quoted higher and some sales
have been made at the advance, but Territory
wools have had the bulk of the business for
many months past. Washed fleeces are quoted
about 1 cent higher in sympathy with other
: wools. Pulled wools hold firm, with prices
hardening on the finer grades, but coarser
wools are yet quieter.
Quotationsâ Unwashed medium, etc., Mis
souri quarter blood combing and clothing, 21@
22c; three-eighths blood, 21*@22*c. Territory
wools â Utah, Wyoming, fine medium, 15@17c;
scoured. 47<&50c: Montana and Dakota, fine me

HIDES, TALLOW, WOOL AND HOPS.
Hops can be sold at 12%0 for future delivery,
but the growers decline to accept this figure.
The advance in Wool has checked the de
mand, and the market Is quieter. Several de
scriptions are marked up, as will be seen.
The circular of Sumner &. Co. says of Hides.
"The Hide business has been quite acme
during the last month. Sales have been made
readily at advanced prices, and tanners have
bought freely, and as the inferior and lower
grades of Hides have been shipped to the east
ern market, there is little or no surplus stock
in the market. Harness and skirting leather
have 'been selling readily for the past few
months, and the better grade of Hides those
that are well taken off and free from brands
and damage, command liberal values. Â«et
Salted Heavy Steers are in demand _ for har
ness and. skirting leather. Wet Salted Medium
and Light Steers are active. Wet Salted Cows
tell readily at full prices. Wet baited Kip
Veal and Calf are very firm and rule at tun
rates. Dry Hides are firm. The prices for Dry
Flint Hides are very close to the values which
are obtainable In the East. Deer Skin* are
more active and values are made at higher ng
ures than before. We quote the summer and
medium skins in demand, and the winter poor
and thin skins are fairly active. Goat Skins ,
are very quiet, but small quantities come to
this market, and at present rew Goat bKins
as a rule are tanned in California.
â¢â¢During the past month Tallow has been
somewhat weaker, owing to large supplies com
ing in from California and the neighboring lo
calities. The market, however, la well sus
tained and prices firm, and In all probability
the present values will continue for some time

In sacks; Pennsylvania Anthracite Ess. Â»14
t'annel, t* 50 per ton; Rock Springs and Castle
Gate. $7 60; Coke, $12 per ton In bulk and $14
in sacks. .. Â
Harrison's circular says: "Since the Ala
meda left the following vessels have deliv
ered coal cargoes from Australia: Benares,
2365 tons; Springburn, 4131 tons; Amiral Troude,
2577 tons; Kufua E. Wood, 2120 tons; Hecla,
2132 tons; total. 13,325 tons. The number of
vessels chartered in Australia to load coal for
here Is about the same as when the last mall
left, footing up about 92,000 tons capacity. A
number of these will not arrive here this year.
The principal activity in the fuel line for the
moment Is the demand from Honolulu, engage
ments having been made this week for sev
eral vessels to load Colonial coal for Honolulu
into May. 1900. This strongly evidences the
scarcity of tonnage for early loading there,
and will divert orders to the British Columbia
collieries for early loadings to Hawaii, for
which extreme prices will be procurable. Busi
ness locally Is fair, and prices are well sus
tained. Coal freights from Newcastle and Syd
ney are firmly held, so that any cheap fuel
from those sections cannot be looked for for
several months at least. Outward grain freights
are strengthening, which should in ordinary
seasons cause an Influx of low priced coals,
but there seems to be a very active demand
for tonnage all over the world, hence this will
not be the year for low freights from any
sections." ' . '

|73 in the afternoon.

trading. The telegram from the pump said:

ously. The water rose during the last twenty
four hours and fell again. At 6:30 this morn
ing it was nineteen Inches deep on the floor
of the 1950-foot level station and going down.'

At a special meeting of the stockholders or
the Ivanhoe Mining Company on Monday it
was voted to diminish the capital stock from
$250,000. divided Into 2500 shares of the par
value of $100 each, to $2*oo, divided Into 2dOO
shares of the par value of $1 each. *
The official letter from the Sierra Nevada
mine for the past week states that on the 140
--foot level the south drift from the main west
drift was extended during the past week 1.
feet, making its total length 150 feet. The face
Is In clay and porphyry-. They have completed
repairs where needed and started west cross
cut No. 2, opposite east crosscut No. 2. and
advanced the same 8 feet. The face Is In
quartz and porphyry- No work was done in
the mine on July 4 and 5. 900-foot levelâ The
I north drift from west crosscut. No. 2 has been
extended during the week 5 feet, making a
i total of 100 feet. The face Is In porphyry and
clay.
An application was made yesterday to have
the stock of the Makawell Sugar Company
listed on the Bond Exchange. The company
has a capital of $5.000, 000 In 100,000 shares of
the par value of $50 each, of which 35,000 full
paid shares have been issued, the remainder
being held. The directors are James T. Alex
ander. H. P. Baldwin, I. Strassburger, Wll-
I Ham Alexander, H. F. Allen and W. H. Chick
erlng. The new company has already declared
a dividend of 40 cents per share, which will be
paid on the 15th. The stock sold at $47 50 yes
terday.
At a special meeting of the committee on
cheap power of the Comstock Pumping Asso
ciation yesterday Charles E. Dutcher, chief
electrician of the Rlsdon staff of engineers in
charge of the pumping plant at the ('. and C.
I shaft, who is also a member of the above com
mittee, was instructed to go to the Truckee
River and inspect the place where parties who
have offered to supply the Comstock mines
with cheap power propose to erect generating
plants.

NOTEâ In the above exposition of the tides
j the early morning tides are given in the left
] hand column and the successive tides of the
j day In the order of occurrence as to time. ThÂ»
second time column gives the second tide of
the day, the third time column the third tide
â  and the last or right hand column gives the
last tide of the day. except when there are but
three tides, as sometimes occur. The heights
given are additions to the soundings on the
United States Const Survey charts, except
j when a minus sign â ) precedes the height,
| and then the number given is subtracted from
the depth given by the charts. The plane of
Reference Is the mean of the lower low waters.
