LIFE SECRET
REVEALED BY
A BURGLARY
A Deserted Wife in
Distant England.
Penitence and Affection Breathe in
Every Line of a Letter Thrown
1 Away by the
Thief.
Oakland Office San Francisco Call,
90S Broadway, July 11.
"â¢That's a chapter of ray life that I
wished to never hear of again." said
Harry Kelly as he walked into the police
station to-night.
And the uphappy romance was all
brought to light by a little robbery that
took place at the Dewey Theater five
mor.ihs ago.
This afternoon a bundle of letters and
an old coat were found near Adams
Wharf. It was at once assumed that they
belonged to some one who had committed I
suicide and were taken to the police sta
tion, where policemen and reporters
delved into the secrets of the owner, who
was supposed to be at the bottom of the
bay. The story of the mystery of the
wharf was soon heard of by Harry Kelly,
property man of the Dewey Theater. He
at once detected som^ similarity between
the recovered articles and - me he had
!'-.st months ago. Especially he remem
bered a letter holding the secret of two
lives and commencing "Dearest Harry."
One glance at the apparent trash was
enough for Kelly. He at once identified
it as that which' was stolen from him.
One Of the letters possessed a strange
fascination for him. He looked over it
carefully and said: "That Is from my
wife. I wanted that chapter of my life
kept a secret."
And this is the letter:
3S Chalkfarm Road. London. N. W.
Dearest Harry: I was so glad to
â¢ hear from yon. I have been worried
while you are all those miles away.
Th^re Is no telling what is happening
to you. The enclosed I cut out of a
paper, and nothing could make me
forget it, for I thought it must be the i
place- where you were. I have gone
aeaily silly thinking about it. Often
'. lav and think of you and cry nearly
tll night, and then I feel so ill and
worn out in the morning, and there is
no or.c I can speak to about it only
Lizzie. You ask me if,l am ill. I am
in mind, and that i? worse than being
ill in body. I am not bodily 111. but I
have r.ot the strength I used to have.
I want to ask you something. Dear,
will you come to me? Only think,
Harry, what It would mean "â  us
bothâ an end to all this misery of be
ing- rated. Picture to yourself a
comfortable home with a loving wife
to do all for you that she is able to
do* and more even, to make up for all
past wrong. For my sake, dear, give
me some hope. Send me a promise.
I will help you all -I can. Do take care
of yourself, and I will try to do as you
tell me and cheer up. but I shall watch '
for a letter. I should like any style
of photo. Do write by return mail.
Harry, if I could only put my arms
around your neck and kiss you a_s I
used to, I think you would grant the
wish of your ever-loving wife.
EMMA.
With a little encouragement â  Harry
Kei'y told the rest of the unhappy ro
mance. In 1592 he and his wife parted I
"She did not do right." he said, "and I
shall never go back. It was not my fault.
In her letter she hints at the trouble, but
it is no use. I wanted to forget that."
"But she admits it and offers to make
amends.' some burly policeman sug
gested.
"Yes, but I shall never go back," said
Kelly, who has a strong London accent.
He has an honorable discharge from the
London poll force and one from the
British army.
COLGAN REFUSES TO
SEND COIN FOR TEACHERS
OAKLAND, July Alameda County
schoolteachers may have to wait for their
pay. The deadlock is due to Assessor
Dalton's action in deducting about $600
from the taxes due the State, claiming the
money as commissions due him.
The situation is an interesting one and
will probably have to go to the Supreme
Court, unless the Assessor yields up the
withheld money under protest. Mr. Col
fan will not settle with Alameda Count
because be claims more coin than the
County Treasurer tendered. Alameda
owes the State $61,342, but the Controller
refused to receive it when County Treas
urer Feidler took It to Sacramento. It
â xas brought back to Oakland and is still
in the county treasury.
Yesterday State School Superintendent
Kirk apportioned the money to the vari
ous counties. Alameda's share Is JSI.TSI,
and Mr. Colon has refused to draw the
â warrant until Alameda County has paid
the full amount which he declares is due.
Mr. Dalton says that his attorneys have
sd vised him that he is entitled by law to
the commissions he has deducted and he
proposes to retain them.

HANNA NOT IN DISFAVOR.
NEW YORK, July 11.â A special to
the Herald from the Isle of Shoal?. N.
H.. says: H. C. Payne, the Wisconsin
member of the Republican National
Committee, said to-day when asked
about Senator Hanna:
"I have observed some discussion in
the press in connection with Senator
Hanna on the management of the next
Republican national campaign, the in
timation being thrown out that there Is
some feeling against him in the na
tional committee. If there is I am not
aware of it. There has been no meet
ing of the national committee, hence no
opportunity for official discussion, and
anything that is said concerning the
sentiment of the committee is purely
conjectural. I have no sympathy with
the criticisms or attacks that have
been made on Senator Hanna. I would
not want to be chairman of the com
mittee."

SHE MAY COME
BACK TO SEEK
BLOODY REVENGE
Madame Montague Is
Coming West.
Oakland Office San Francisco Call.
MC Broadway, July 11.
Madame Florence Montague, the very
romantic lecturer and former president of
the Oakland Psychic Society, is expected
to return to the coast in a few weeks. One
of her friends, with whom she has con- â 
tinually corresponded, says that the ma-l
ame, who has been to England, might be :
here within a month.
When Madame Montague-Yates arrives
here there is likely to be trouble. Since
she left she has, according to her own
statements, been divorced from Mr. Yates
against her will. There is reason to be
lieve, however, that the madame. when
making these statements, as she had done
very forcibly in her correspondence, has
been romancing, for since her sensational
letter was published in The Call a few \
months Ago, the fees of the attorneys and
the court connected with her divorce pro- i
ceedings have all been paid, which would
not have been the case had the lady not ;
consented.
Am matters now stand, according I
madame's signed statement, she has re
joined her husband from whom she has
been divorced, and she Is harboring
vengeance against those who manipulate!
the separation. Acquaintances of hers in
Oakland and San Francisco, who know
her well, think that her remarkable letter
berating her former friends and declar
ing that Judge Hall was induced to issue
a decree by reason of fraud, must have
been written subject to some "inspira
tion" which does not conform with the
facts.
Madame Montague's attornoy. George
de Golia. says that the legal bisiness has
been closed up and the lady has settled ;
ail the expenses of the contest and that j
he is totally at a losjs to understand what !
she meant by her strange statements.
Should the madame return to Oakland |
her homecoming will be the signal for
, some lively incidents, and it has been pre- {
dieted that her excitable French dispo- j
sition may prompt her to do a little gun- i
nlng. y

OAKLAND POLICE
ARE PUZZLING
OVER A MYSTERY
Jack Stanton's Skull
Crushed.
Injured Man Walks to the Hospital,
but His Lips Are Sealed and
He Is Unable to Give Ac
count of the Affair.
"Oakland Office San Francisco Call,
SQB Broadway, July 11.
An air of deepest mystery, surrounds
the case of Jack Stanton, a roaming
waiter and cook, who at about 7 o'clock i
this morning walked into the Receiving
Hospital to be treated for a compound j
fracture of the skull.
How he sustained the injury that may
prove fatal is not known. When asked
by Steward Borchert, Stanton tried to
speak, but his lips seemed sealed, for the
injury to his brain had affected the nerve
controlling the power of speech, and he
seemed semi-unconscious, though he pos
sessed the presence of mind to hasten to
'. the place where relief might be afforded
him.
: On a slip of paper he slowly wrote these
words rather unintelligibly: "runaway
was in Oakland. J. Stanton." Then his
strength failed him and he dropped the
pencil.
From a wound at the left side of the
head bicod trickled down and was satu
rating his clothes. The case so puzzled
the hospital steward that the latter
promptly summoned Dr. Porter and later
Drs. Milton and Rowe.
After Stanton was put under the influ
ence of anesthetics Dr. Porter laid open
the scalp, when the seriousness of the
Injuri23 appeared. The doctors found very
little hemorrhage. The skull was badly
fractured.- while the wound through the
scalp appeared only as large as a silver
quarter. There was also a slight wound
on the top of his head, It was found nec
essary to remove several pieces of the
shattered bone and about an ounce of
brain matter. The operation lasted over
two hours and the doctors express little
hope for his recovery.
There are many conjectures as to how
Stanton received' his injury, which bears
the appearance of having been inflicted
with a hammer. Then again, it is thoiight
that he fell out of a wagon, his head
striking upon a sharp, small rock. Others
go so far as to say that the element of
murder may be involved.
Stanton was later in the day identified
by Dave Smith, a waiter at the Philadel
: phia restaurant on Broadway, where the
i injured man had been employed in the
same capacity several months ago.
"Stanton was a drinking man," said
Smith "and when in his cups he was ex
ceedingly quarrelsome. I feel almost sure
that his injury is the result of an early
morning right. I had trouble with him
at the restaurant when he worked there
for a short time and that's why he quit
Chief of Police Hodgkins has detailed
Detectives Holland and Quigley to inves
tigate the case, but after working all day
on the matter report it difficult to gather
any facts. No runaways have been heard
of and thus far no one has been found
who can give any facts further than those
already related.
Stanton is about -4 years of age. It is
said that he has a wife living in this city,
but the name "J. Stanton" does not ap
pear in the directory, and he is supposed
to have come here recently from Santa
Cruz, where his father is a printer on the
I Sentinel, and where his mother conducts
the Duncan House. Judging from the
i notes in a diary, found on Stanton, giving
a record of his doings and travels, he has
been wandering all over the State. In his
pockets were also found a comb, cake of
soap, razor and strap, in short, the com
plete outfit of a wanderer of the road.
At the hospital he appeared in a neat
suit of clothes and there was no dirt or
dust to indicate that he had rolled in the
roadway. ,
GAINS FOR SADLER.
Nevada Governorship Contest Near-
ing Its Close.
CARSON, Nev.. July 11.â The testi
mony on both sides in the Governor
ship contest was finished this morning
before the Supreme Court. The attor
neys have been anxious for the nmrt
to appoint a day for the re-exait'un.i
tion of the ballots objected to. The
court appointed Thursday for the re
examination. This will occupy several
days. It is expected the court will
render a partial decision as the count
ing progresses. '-â â ', - : -.'-'
During the case Sadler gained four
votes not objected to. and McMillan
three. At the close of the case Sadler
has a clean majority of sixty votes
over McMillan. Sadler has filed 365 ob
jections to ballots claimed to be irreg
ular; McMillan objected to 200 ballots.
The ballots are in the hands of the

CORPORATIONS
ARE CITED TO
SHOW CAUSE
Must Explain Small
Tax Payments.
WELLS-FARGO HOTLY GRILLED
SUPERVISORS SIT AS A BOARD
OF EQUALIZATION.
Protests Against Erroneous Assess
ments Coming la in Bunches,
but in Most Cases Assess
or's Figures Stand.
The banks and quasi-public corpora
tions are to be hard put to it to explain
just why they should not shoulder their
just -share of the taxes that are this year
to be paid into this city and county. It j
| is the opinion of Assessor Dodge that he
; has about everything assessed that can
j be touched under the law or which has
not been converted into drafts and de
i posited temporarily with foreign eorre
j spondents of local banking institutions,
but the Supervisors have an idea that
they may find a way to go him one bet
ter, and to this end they yesterday
adopted a resolution citing about every
banking institution, public or private,
and money broker of importance in this
city to appear before them while sitting
as a Board of Equalization and show
cause why their assessments shall not
go up.
"Tackle it if you want to," said Asses
sor Dodge to them yesterday. "I have
spent the better part of a month in go
ing over their books, and in some easeg
i they have openjy laughed at me. I have
got them assessed as close as they can
! be, considering, of course, legal exemp
j tions and their ability to evade taxation
ion certain classes of collateral. Its the
I private banking firms we are afraid of.
The man around the corner who may be
using $3,000,000 or $4,000,000 in his busi
ness but who renders a statement to me
of only $100,000, the law does not give
me the right to inspect his books and I
j can do nothing with him."
The board agreed with Assessor
Dodge that he probably had every
thing charged against the corpora
i tlona that he could find, but at the
I same time thought it would be as
j well to have them all up and question
; them as to their franchises. Consequent
ly Mr. Algeltinger offered a resolution
calling upon them to appear on the morn
ing of the 14th and show cause. Only
Lackmann and Heyer voted against it.
Assessor Dodge made one exception in
his list of untouchable banks. It was
that philanthropic institution owned and
operated by Wells, Fargo & Co. The
Wells-Fargo people, although' maintain
ing their headquarters in San Francisco,
are Incorporated under the laws of Colo
rado. They have branches in New York,
Utah and Portland. They have a sur
| plus of $6,712,000 on a capital of $500,000.
j but that vast amount is farmed out
I among their branches and cannot be
! taxed in this State, notwithstanding that
the company's chief place of business is
here, for the reason that under the law
such funds can only be taxed in the home
I of the corporationâ the place under the
laws of which it is incorporated.
""Wells-Fargo's president only laughed
at us," said Mr. Dodge, "and he told me
that he could come in here next year
and not pay a cent in taxes unless he
wanted to, and I guess he is pretty near
right. They simply unload their taxable
holdings among their branches and we
cannot touch them. All that we can as
sess them on here is real estate to the
tune of $2,172,947 and cash, which they
have notified us amounts to $496,099."
The Assessor said that there was just
one way of getung at the company and
that was by raising the assessment on
their banking franchise, which he has
already assessed at $10,000. and this prop
osition the board will consider.
In considering protests against this
year's assessments the board is for fhe
most part backing up Mr. Dodge's fig
ures, although few reductions have yet
been requested. Nearly all of yesterday
was given up to considering reductions
ranging from $50 to half as many thou
sand.
Two representatives of the Callagham
Estate Company came in to order a re
duction in a valuable triangular piece of
| property at West Mission and Potter
j streets. It was assessed last year at
$125,000; this year Mr. Dodge voluntarily
reduced it to $115,000. The estate wanted
it cut down to $57.000 and bandied not
many words in making its wants known.
Investigations developed that there was
every probability that the city owned the
| property and that the holders were little
I more than squatters. The assessment
was promptly raised to last year's figures
and the Callaghams retired disgruntled.
Property owners, or plausible rep
i resentatives, came to the front to
I swear glibly that their holdings were
not worth half the assessment, but
when asked as to their insurance it
developed that in most cases they car
ried policies for amounts far in excess
of the assessment. As insurance com
panies take risks only on about 60 per
cent of the actual valuation of the prop
erty, there was nothing left for the board
to do but deny. the petitions.
The Columbia Savings and Loan So
ciety sent in a representative w T ho was
loaded up with unkind things to be shot
at the Assessor. Dodge assessed the so
ciety's franchise at $2500, and the soci
ety's representative said that when he
i asked the Assessor upon what he had
based his calculation that official was
unable to tell him and referred him to
I "his expert." "If he based it upon the
number of our depositors." said he, "then
the assessment of the Hioernia Bank
should be raised a hundred or so times
what it is to be in proportion." The so
ciety's kick was well delivered, notwith
standing rather a severe struggle with
! modern English on the part of the ora
' tor. but it had no effect upon the board
I and the assessment remained at the origi
nal $2500.
The board will continue its considera
tion of property at 10 o'clock this morn
ing. _________Â» _

DECLINE TO
ACT WITH THE
HEALTH BOARD
Harbor Commission
ers Have No Power.
CONEMAUGH SAILS FOR MANILA
QUARANTINED PASSENGERS OF
NIPPON MARU LANDED.
Elaborate Preparations on the Front
for the Reception of Oregon
TToops â First Water
melons of Season.
The Harbor Commissioners yesterday
decided to take no action on the request
of the local Board of Health for their
co-operation in refusing to allow vessels
! to dock unless they had a clean bill of
health. Two weeks ago a communication
was received relating to the subject,
which was referred to Attorney Budd for
his advice as to the powers of the Com
missioners in the matter, and he recom
mended that the communication be filed.
He argued that the Harbor Board could
not act except in a specific case whera
there was no doubt of the presence of
infectious diseases on the vessels and that
the Federal, State and municipal health
authorities were competent to deal with
the contingency.
Commissioner Kilburn presented the
names of two men for appointment as
collectors and Commissioners Herold and
Harney voted favorably. Eugene Sulli
van, former Assemblyman of the Forty
fifth District, succeeds G. A. Murphy, and
â  M. H. Barry, former Assemblyman of the
P'orty-fourth. took J. M. Nashs place.
The terms of the displaced officers have
both expired.
The transport Conemaugh left yester
day afternoon for Manila with 276 horses
for the Fourth Cavalry. lieutenant Wi
nans and a squad of thirty-five men are
in charge of the animals. The latter are
j ranged along the sides of the vessel in
small stalls and will be given a chance to
' stretch themselves at Honolulu.
The fifty-four liberated passengers of
the Nippon Maru came over from quaran
tine yesterday morning on the steamer
Caroline. Captain W. G. Leale landed
the happy band at Jackson-street wharf,
I where three cheers were given for Dr.
! Kinyoun, Federal Quarantine Officer. The
| Governor Perkins met them off Lombard
street wharf and local Quarantine Officer
Colin stamped their health certificates.
The transport City of Para will leave
to-morrow with the headquarters band,
four companies of the Twenty-fourth In
fantry and two troops of the Fourth
Cavalry. The Tartar leaves on the 22d
with 1100 men of the Nineteenth Infantry.
The Tacoma will take on 200 horses in
about three days.
The bark Santiago, the first of the over
due sugar packets, arrived yesterday
morning, thirty days from Hilo, with four
I passengers. She went to Long wharf,
i Oakland, to discharge her cargo.
Angus Beaton, a re-enlisted soldier
! waiting for assignment, was brought to
the Harbor Hospital yesterday to be
treated for an ugly wound on the right
side of the head. Beaton, who was per
fectly sober, claims that he was struck
on the head With a revolver by Officer
Davis, a new member of the police force,
I who was not in uniform, and without any
provocation. He says he was walking
I along near the Hotel Nymphia with a
friend, another soldier, whom Davis
! kicked and followed it up by striking
I Beaton. The latter was charged with
I disturbing the peace. Dr. Rice dressed
his wound.
Four minor cases were treated at the
Harbor Hospital. E. Moore, a teamster,
sustained a compound fracture of the
hand v.hile loading a boiler on Mission
i and East streets. A. Haven had a piece
of steel extracted from his right index
finger and Joseph H. Muller had his left
index finger ground off by an emery
! wheel at the Daw Strain Pump "Works.
Edward Lynch was arrested by Officers
I Dalton and Crosby on suspicion of- hav
| ing broken Robert Hamilton's leg on,
! Fisherman's wharf last Sunday in an al
tercation. Lynch was charged with may
hem, but says he knows nothing of the
assault and was not there when It oc
curred.
The first watermelons of the season ar
rived yesterday from Fresno, and Com
mission Merchant McDonough was en
riched $12.50 thereby. His partner, Run
| yan, bet that none would arrive before
July 13, and he lost by two days.
In addition to the tug Governor Irwin,
which will take Adjutant General Sen
mans ' and his staff out to the incoming
transports Ohio and Newport when they
arrive, the tug Sea Queen has been gen
erously donated by Captain AY". J. Gray
to convey the Native Sons' committee.
Colonel W. P. Sullivan, who will repre
j sent the Mayor, and the members of the
! press. The band provided by the Mayor
will also accompany the party, which will
leave from Mission-street wharf. Due no
tice will be given through the telephone
and by the blowing of whistles and siren 3
as soon as the vessels are sighted, and
there will be time enough given to reach
the wharf.
An eight-inch whistle was placed on the
Yolo Mills by Adolph Hinz Jr.. who had
the tip of his" finger cut off at the Harbor
Hospital as a result of his enthusiasm.
The whistle will blow at the appointed
time and be heard clear to Alameda.

reception of the Oregon troops is entirely
due to the press committee, who worked
unremittingly for it. The members ar*

CRUEL TO HIS CHILDREN.
Otto Gross, a Tailor, Accused of Mis
treatment â Eureka Society to
Investigate.
Otto Gross, a tailor, with place of busi
ness at 1217 Polk street, will be prosecuted
by the Eureka Society for the Prevention
of Cruelty to Children if he does not con
tribute to the care of his children by hi 3
divorced wife. The latter brought Marie,
aged six, and Carl, aged eight, to the
rooms of the society yesterday and
showed Secretary Wadham their meager
appearance and wretched clothing. She
undressed both children to show the terri
ble condition of their bodies, due to the
ill care given them by their father, with
whom they have been living for the past
year. The mother stated she had been
divorced from Gross for over a year and
although she wa3 awarded the custody of
the children and $32 a month alimony, she
allowed Gross to have them, he promising
to feed, clothe and educate them prop
erly
That he did not carry out his part of the
contract was apparent in the condition of
the little ones. The little girl is suffering
from blood poisoning brought on by being
vaccinated, the dirt being allowed to lodge
in the little one's arm. Their mother
claims that Gross used to lock them up in
the back of his store and go downtown.
During his absence the children would lie
in bed and rob their hearts out for a pro
tector. The mother learned of their plight
and although she is poor, has taken them
in hand and will do her utmost to bring
them back to health. Secretary Wadham
s-tates he will visit the cruel parent to
day and if he refuses to contribute to the
support of the children he will swear to
a warrant and take him Into custody.

GEORGE GREEN IN
THE ROLE OF HERO
DANNY KEEDHAM had a narrow escape from drowning out at the
ocean beach, where he Is training George Green for his coming fight
with Charlie Goff, yesterday morning, and the fact that he is still In
the land of the living is due to Green's nerve, combined with rare
presence of mind and flrst-clasÃ. swimming ability. Green's training quarters
are located at the Seal Rock House, and each day before starting in to
work off his surplus fiit and harden his muscles for the coming battle it has
been his custom to take a stroll along the beach in company with his
trainer Yesterday morning, immediately after breakfast, the two athletes
started from the"ir training quarters tt>r the usual hour's lounging. They
walked out to the end of the Olympic Club pier, and while Needham pre
pared to catch a few fish Green sought a comfortable seat and was soon
engrossed in a perusal of Kipling's "Vampire."
Needham dropped the line overboard and waited patiently for a bltft. The
fish were shy, however, and after fifteen minutes the amateur fisherman
hauled in his line for a fresh cast. The splashing surf had soaked the string
ers of the pier, making the footing rather precarious, and as Needham made
his cast he stepped on the edge of the slippery structure and the next instant
was pitched headlong into the water. As he disappeared from view an in
voluntary co' escaped from his lips, and Green looked up just in time to
catch a glimpse of his vanishing form. Knowing Needham to be an expert
swimmer, Green was not greatly alarmed, thinking his trainer would experi
ence no difficulty in rescuing himself from his predicament. But there was
a big sea on, and when Needham came to the surface and struck out for the
pier a huge wave caught him and dashed him with considerable violence
against the side of the structure, rendering him unconscious.
Green saw his daager and as Needham sank beneath the waves he
stripped off his coat and plunged in after him. A moment later rescued
and rescuer came to the surface together some distance from the pier.
Needham was still unconscious and it required all Green's skill as a swim
mer and all his strength to keep himself and his helpless companion afloat.
After a heroic battle, with the waves and the outrunning tide, he succeeded
in reaching the pier -with his burden. Holding on to the pier with one hand
and keeping Needham afloat with the other. Green called lustily for help.
At that hour of the day there are very few people in the vicinity and his
cries were unanswered. Finally, -when almost exhausted by the strain of
"his exertions, (Jreen succeeded In restoring Needham to consciousness and a
moment later both were safe on the pier. They hurried to the Seal Rock
House, and Needham waa given a strong dose of whisky and put to bed.
Green was none the worse for his exciting experience, and after a good rub
down went for his usual spin along the beach.
A few hours' rest sufficed to put Needham on his feet and in the after
noon he was about as usual, declaring that he felt all right. This makes
the fourth escape from a watery grave for Needham within a year. Last
summer while on his trip to Alaska he was twice pulled out of the Yukon,
and again at Dutch Harbor he was rescued from drowning by a hair's
breadth Green's bravery and coolness under such trying circumstances
have doubled the admiration of his multitude of friends and added many
more to the list. He modestly disclaims being entitled to any particular
credit but the fact remains, and is generally recognized, ihat he performed
a feat that has been the making of more than one hero in the past, and
those who were informed of the occurrence yesterday were enthusiastic In
singing his praises.

Miss Sloan's Address

STARTED HER IN BUSINESS
LODGEBS GLARED AT HER SO
SHE FLED THE TOWN.
Superintendent of an Alaska Com
pany Buys a Lodging House for
a Woman Who Skips With
the Coin-
L. W. Smith, superintendent of an
Alaska company, has been inquiring for
Miss Myrtle Sloan's address. Smith has
a touching note from the fickle damsel,
asking him to' write, but as she failed
to mention her street and number, he
has been obliged to seek police assistance
so that he may respond to her request.
This; is the second time within the past
few weeks that the millionaire superin
tendent has had to apply to the sleuths to
find the lady. â¢ .
A few months ago Myrtle rushed up
north to an old flame, George Barrett
who had just been released after a long
trial for the killing of Frank \\ ill lama
in Portland. Smith followed the faithless
damsel, found her in Seattle and Induced
her to return here with him.
Shortly after Myrtles return, Barrett
came to town, and also engaged apart
ments at 613 Bush street. Barrett s pres
ence developed in Miss Sloan a decided
business taste and she induced Smith to
purchase from John Ellis the lodging
house at 842 Post street, paying lor the
same $1000 and giving Smith a chattel
mortgage on the house as security for his
Myrtle figured as a landlady but a few
days and within the very week she pur
chased the house she sold it back to
Harris for $9uo and disappeared with Bar
rett and the coin.
When Smith discovered that he no
longer owned a lodging-house, he sought
the assistance of Captain Bohen, who de
tailed Detective George Grahnm on the
case. Graham did excellent work In the
matter and in short order located Barrett
and through him the woman. Smith met
Myrtle in the captain's oflicv. At Smith s
invitation, Myrtle went with him to din
ner to talk the maÂ«ter over. No definite
arrangement was made, and it was de
cided that Smith and the woman meet
again the next day at dinner and con
clude the business in hand.
The dinner grew cold and the lady came
not. The night grew late and still no
lady. Finally Smith returned to his rooms
at "the RUBS, where a note from Myrtle
awaited him. Humbly the writer apolo
gized for not appearing at the dinner, ex
plaining her absence by the fact that
sirice the detectives had ribught her out
at the lodging-house she had been the
observed of all observers and as she was
too modest to stand the rude stare of
strangers, she had left the town. In a
P. S. she added: -'Write soon"
Smith, it is rumored, is still willing to
forget and forgive.
When seen last evening Mr. Smith ad
mitted that he knew Miss Sloan. He said
she was a Portland girl and was a mem
ber of a well-known family in that city.
He said he was an unmarried man and
could go with any woman he. pleased.
"1 may have given her money at times."
he continued, "but it is my business. I
never lost any money through her. She
has not run away, but is in Los Angelas.
I do not know whether Mr. Barrett is
with her or not, and I really don't care.
"I am not a los^r In the transaction, nor
do I want her arrested/

THE SEMI-COMMUTER IS TOO
SWIFT FOR C. P. HUNTINGTON
IN the language of the street, C. P.
Huntington is "a wise Indian."
Many times has he ridden the over
land trail and never yet has he
been detected by a lost quarter or
a single scent. For years he has har
ried the gentle savages of the Com
muter tribe resident in Alameda Coun
ty and ever and anon he has cinched
the suffering San Francisco public un
til it has come to be accepted as a
fact that the aged S. P. chief is the
cleverest manipulator of wampum
alive.
Let him prepare to darken his Fifth
avenue tepee and weep. A newer and
greater financier has been found. Let
the .subchiefs and the braves in the
yellow wigwam on Montgomery street
eat crow' Let Goodman, and Millsy,
and Kru'ttsy and "Fatty" Fillmore
take to the tall timber and dig roots.
The old man will need room when he
hears the news and woe betide the
feathered head that comes within reach
of his scalping knife. Z-z-z-i-i-p it
will swingâ and you know what that
means. , â¢ ,
The semi-commuter is at the bot
tom of it all. You will not find him
in the dictionary or in any scientific
book. He was discovered too recently
AN AMADOR
MAN FINDS HIS
LONG LOST SON
Duggan's Search Is
Ended.
MEETING AFTER MANY YEARS
EACH THOUGHT THE OTHEB.
LONG SINCE DEAD.
A Casual Remark Leads to the Meet
ing of "Billy George" and
the Author of His
Being.
A father and his son,, strancrers for
twenty-five years, each considering the
other dead, met a few evenings ago in
this city. The father is Theodore C. Dug
gan, one of the solid citizens of Plymoiun,
Amador County, and proprietor of two
stage lines running from that point. His
newly discovered son, who has long gone
by the name of Willi.tm George, is better
known to the one-time frequenters of the
old Baldwin billiard rooms as "Baldwin
George."
The meeting was arranged by James
Orndorf, one of the proprietors of the
Baldwin Annex, and took place last Fri
day in front of the bar of that resort.
"Billy," said Orndorf, presenting the
young man to an elderly gentleman, "this
is your long lost father. Mr. Duggan,
your son, Billy George!"
The reunited pair clasped hands, took
a good look at each other and shook.
"Have a drink." said the long-lost
father, releasing the hand of his new
found son long enough to reach into his
pocket for the price.
"Gimme a' little rye," said the young
man in response to the inquiry of the bar
tender as to the nature of his tipple.
"What's that?" exclaimed the father,
hastily placing on the bar the bottle from
which he had just poured a couple of stiff
lingers. "What's that!"
"A little rye," replied the young fellow.
Orndorf couid see from the glint in the
eye of the elder man that ho did not like
the Idea that his son was drinking whisky.
He saw th:u the happy reunion might be
disrupted if he did not act promptly.
"Don't you worry," he said to Duggan,
"because your son is drinking a little
whisky. I've known him for. fifteen years
and never saw the sign of liquor on him
yet. You two just go over there, sit down
and have a good talk. You'll find the boy
knows how to take care of himself.
Father and son Bat down at one of the
tables. They had not been talking two
minutes before Orndorf raw I'ug.ean ex
tend his arms and with tears streaming
from his eyes clasp the young man to
his breast. Time and again during the
half hour they sat there the father was
visibly affected, and when he arose to
accompany his son to meet an entirely
unsuspected daughter-in-law and two
grandchildren, he was as proud as Punch.
On the way to the home on Sixth street
he hecamo BO demonstrative in his af
fection for the newly discovered son that
a policeman was on the point of arrest
ing him. The bluecoat did stop them, but
satisfied with their explanation of the
reason for the Joy allowed them to pro
ceed on their way. Duggan met his son's
wife and hugged his children and spent
{ill of (Saturday with the family. return-
Ing to his home at Plymouth Sunday. He
would have taken the entire family with
him had not Mrs. George been too ill to
stand the journey.
The separation of the elder and younger
Duggan took place at Monterey more
than a quarter of a century ago, as the
result of the divorce of his father and
mother. Duggan left for parts unknown
to his former wife, and the boy, upon the
remarriage of his mother to a man named
George, took the surname of his step
father. The father went to Amador
County and there married a Miss Millie
Hayden, who is the mother of his two
other sons and two daughters. The boy,
as tha years passed, considered his father
dead. He wandered over the face of the
country, locating permanently In this city
some five years ago. From time to time
for that. although it is probable he
has existed for more than a year. As
near as can be ascertained, he is a
half-breed, a cross between the resi
dent of Oakland or Alameda who
daily crosses the ferry to this, city and
the San Francisco public. He looks
and acts and moves like other men,
but is not. He is a Frankenstein
growth of conditions established by
Huntington and has returned to de
stroy his master.
The method of the semi-commuter
is simple. In the lirst place, he lives
on this side of the bay, but has busi
ness that compels his presence in Oak
land or Alameda a dozen times a
month. Twelve round trips at 20 cents
figure up $2 40, which the semi-com
muter dislikes to drop in the S. P.
wampum belt. Wherefore, he has set
his cunning to work and now has mat
ters so planned that, while he makes
the necessary journeys across the fer
ries in the choo-choo canoes, he never
pays more than two-thirds of the
tribute.
First of all, the semi-commuter, in
tending to go to Oakland or Alameda,
buys a one-way red tinted ticket to
West Berkeley. That's 10 cents. As
he passes through the depot the gate
keeper punches two F's in the ticket,
â which is not taken up, as the fatuous
S. P. people imagine the wayfarer will
present it to the conductor on the

The semi-commuter takes any old
kind of clean paper and reduces It to
a pulp. This pulp is forced into the
F punch holes and pressed firmly in
place. When it is dried, a little red
Ink colors the supplied parts with a
color so similar to that of the remain
der of the ticket that, if his patching
is well done, it can be used over and
over again, a saving of 10 cents each
time.

Francisco. Let Huntingto-n weep when
he casts up the figures. A thousand
semi-commuters paying $1 50 a month
when they should pay $2 40 is a clear
loss to him, at 90 cents for each, of
$900 a month.
